آدمی اپنے بیٹے کے بدلے قتل نہیں کیا جائے گا اور نہ اپنے غلام کے بدلے اور نہ اپنے مدبر کے بدلے اور نہ اپنے مکاتب کے بدلے اور اپنے بیٹے کے غلام کے بدلے۔ صلی اللہ باپ اپنے بیٹے کوقتل کر دے تو بیٹے کے بدلے باپ کو قتل نہیں کیا جائے گا بلکہ دیت لازم کی جائے گی ۔ یا آقا نے اپنے غلام کوقتل کر دیا تو غلام کے بدلے آقا کوقتل نہیں کیا جائے گا۔ البتہ دیت لازم ہوگی اور تعزیر کی جائے گی۔ والد کی عزت واحترام ہے اس کی وجہ سے لڑکے کی وجہ سے والد کو قتل نہیں کیا جائے گا (۲) حدیث میں ہے۔ عن ابن عباس عن النبی قال لا تقام الحدود في المساجد ولا يقتل الوالد بالولد (الف) (ترندی شریف، باب ماجاء فی الرجل يقتل ابنه القادمنه ام لاص ۲۵۹ نمبر ۱ ۱۴۰/ ابن ماجہ شریف، باب لا تقتل الوالد بولدہ نمبر (۲۶۶) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ لڑکے کی وجہ سے والد کو قتل نہیں کیا جائے گا۔ اور غلام کی وجہ سے آقا قتل نہیں کیا جائے گا اس کی دلیل یہ حدیث ہے۔ عن عمر بن شعيب عن ابيه عن جده ان رجلا قتل عبده متعمدا فجلده رسول الله الله مائة ونفاه سنة ومحى سهمه من المسلمين. وفي روایت دارقطني ، ولم يقده به وامره ان يعتق رقبة (ب) (دار قطنی ، کتاب الحدود والدیات ج ثالث ص ۱۰۵ نمبر ۳۲۵۴ رابن ماجہ شریف، باب هل يقتل الحر بالعبد؟ ص ۱۳۸۳ نمبر ۲۶۶۴) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ آقا کوقتل نہیں کریں گے بلکہ اس سے دیت لی جائے گی۔ اور غلام ہی کے درجے میں مدبر اور مکاتب ہے۔ اس لئے ان کو قتل کرنے سے بھی آقا پر قصاص نہیں ہے۔ اور لڑکے پر باپ کا احترام ضروری ہے اس لئے لڑے کے غلام کو قتل کرنے سے بھی باپ کو قتل نہیں کیا جائے گا (۲) او پر حدیث گزری انت و مالک لوالدک ان اولادكم من اطيب كسبكم حاشیہ : (الف) آپ نے فرمایا حدود مساجد میں قائم نہ کیا جائے اور نہ والد کولر کے کے بدلے قتل کرے (ب) حضرت عمر بن شعیب فرماتے ہیں کہ ایک آدمی نے جان بوجھ کر اپنے غلام کو قتل کیا تو حضور نے اس کو سو کوڑے لگائے اور ایک سال تک شہر بدر کیا۔ اور مسلمانوں کے مال غنیمت سے اس کا حصہ ختم کر دیا۔ اور دار قطنی کی روایت میں ہے کہ اس سے قصاص نہیں لیا اور اس کو حکم دیا کہ غلام آزاد کرے۔
And a man is not killed for his son, nor for his slave, nor for his mudabbir, nor for his mukatab, nor for his son's slave. A man will not be killed for his son if he kills his son; rather, diya becomes obligatory. Or if a master kills his slave, the master is not killed for the slave; rather, diya becomes obligatory and ta'zir is given. The father has honor and respect, therefore the father is not killed for the son. In a hadith: from Ibn Abbas from the Prophet ﷺ: 'Hudud are not established in mosques, nor is a father killed for his child' (Tirmidhi, Ibn Majah). From this hadith it is understood that the father is not killed for the son. And that the master is not killed for the slave is proven by the hadith of Amr ibn Shu'ayb from his father from his grandfather: a man intentionally killed his slave, so the Messenger of Allah flogged him one hundred lashes, exiled him for a year, and erased his share from the Muslims. And in a narration of al-Daraqutni: 'He did not execute qiṣāṣ from him and ordered him to free a slave.' From this hadith it is known that the master will not be killed; rather, diya will be taken from him. And mudabbir and mukatab are of the same status as a slave, therefore killing them also does not incur qiṣāṣ upon the master. And respect for the father is necessary, therefore killing the son's slave also does not result in the father being killed.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage details exceptions to the general rule of qiṣāṣ: a father is not executed for killing his son, nor a master for killing his slave, mudabbir (slave promised freedom upon master's death), or mukatab (slave under contract of emancipation). The text also extends this to a son's slave, since the son's property effectively belongs to the father. The proofs are: (1) the hadith 'a father is not killed for his child' and (2) the hadith of the man who killed his slave and was punished with flogging, exile, and compensation but not qiṣāṣ. The Hanafi school thus establishes that relationships of direct lineal descent (ascent) and master-slave ownership prevent qiṣāṣ. Instead, the killer must pay diya (blood money) and receive ta'zir (discretionary punishment). The ruling is based on honor of the father and the principle that a man's property is his own.

Key Terms
مدبر (mudabbir): A slave whose manumission is deferred until after the master's death.
مكاتب (mukātab): A slave who has contracted to purchase his freedom from his master.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) A father kills his son.
B) A master kills his mudabbir.
C) A father kills his son-in-law.
D) A master kills his mukatab.

Key Takeaway
A father is not executed for killing his son, and a master is not executed for killing his slave; the penalty shifts to diya and ta‘zir.

Inherited Qiṣāṣ Against Father Falls Away
States the rule and proofs for dropped qiṣāṣ against father.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a son inherits the right to qiṣāṣ against his father, identify the primary evidence (the hadith 'a father is not killed for his child'), and explain why the right drops entirely even when there are multiple heirs.

Core Idea
A son cannot execute qiṣāṣ against his father, even if he inherits the right, due to the prohibition of a parent being killed for a child.

This passage addresses a specific scenario in qiṣāṣ: when the right to demand retribution is inherited by a child against their own parent. The Hanafi school rules that the right to qiṣāṣ drops automatically due to the impediment of paternity. We will examine the ruling, its evidence from hadith and athar, and the application when multiple heirs are involved.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Recognise the situation: a father kills the son's mother; the son becomes an heir to the right of qiṣāṣ against his father.
The key factor is that the killer is an ascendant of the heir.
2
State the ruling
The Hanafi ruling is that the son's right to qiṣāṣ drops; he cannot demand execution.
This is the established position (zahir al-riwayah).
3
Locate the primary dalil
The primary evidence is the hadith 'لا يقتل الوالد بالولد' (a father is not killed for his child), reported by Tirmidhi and Ibn Majah.
This is a zanni al-thubut hadith but qati al-dalalah in meaning.
4
Apply the principle to multiple heirs
If other heirs exist alongside the son, the right drops for all of them, because qiṣāṣ is a single right that drops for all if it drops for some.
Supported by the hadith of restraint and the Umar precedent.
5
Determine the practical outcome
The father is not executed; instead, he must pay diya (blood money) to the heirs and may receive ta'zir punishment.
The diya is the alternative remedy when qiṣāṣ is not available.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن ورث قصاصا على ابيه سقط
کوئی وارث ہو جائے قصاص کا اپنے باپ پر تو وہ ساقط ہو جائے گا۔ مثلا باپ نے بیٹے کی ماںکو قتل کیا جس کی وجہ سے باپ پر قصاص لازم تھا۔ لیکن ماں کے وارث ہونے کی وجہ سے بیٹا قتل کا حقدار تھا اس لئے باپ سے قتل ساقط ہو جائے گا۔ او پر حدیث گزری_لايقاد الوالد بالولد ( ترمذی شریف، نمبر ۱۴۰۰ ابن ماجه شریف، نمبر ۲۶۶) اور اگر بیٹے کے ساتھ دوسرے لوگ بھی وارث تھے تب بھی قتل ساقط ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ بعض اولیاء کی جانب سے قتل ساقط ہو جائے تو پورے کی جانب سے ساقط ہو جائے گا۔ تھے گا۔ کی دلیل یہ حدیث ہے۔ حدثني عائشة ان النبی ال الله قال على المقتتلين ان ينحجزوا الاول فالاول وان كانت امرأة (ب) (سن للبیهقی ، باب عفو بین الاولیاء عن القصاص دون بعض ج نامن، ص ۱۰۵ نمبر ۱۶۰۷۰) (۲) ان عـمـر بـن الخطاب رفع اليه رجل قتل رجلا فاراد اولياء المقتول قتله فقالت اخت المقتول وهى امراة القاتل قد عفوت عن حصتي من زوجي فقال عمر عتق الرجل من القتل (ج) (مصنف عبدالرزاق، باب العفوج عاشر ص ۱۳ نمبر ۱۸۱۸۸) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ وارث اپنا حصہ معاف کر دے تو باقی ورثہ قاتل کو قتل نہیں کر سکتے بلکہ اب دیت لیں گے۔
And whoever inherits qiṣāṣ against his father, it falls. If someone becomes an heir to qiṣāṣ against his father, it will be dropped. For example, the father killed the son's mother, due to which qiṣāṣ was obligatory upon the father. But because the son was an heir of the mother, he became entitled to kill, so the killing against the father drops. The hadith mentioned earlier: 'A father is not killed for his child' (Tirmidhi, Ibn Majah). And if there were other heirs with the son, even then the killing would drop, because if qiṣāṣ drops from the side of some of the guardians, it drops from all. The proof is the hadith: 'The parties to homicide should restrain themselves, the first then the next, even if it is a woman' (Bayhaqi). And also that Umar ibn al-Khattab was presented with a case where a man killed another, and the heirs of the victim wanted to kill him, but the sister of the victim, who was the wife of the killer, said: 'I have forgiven my share from my husband.' So Umar said: 'The man is free from killing.' (Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq). From this athar it is known that if an heir forgives his share, the remaining heirs cannot kill the killer; rather, they will take diya.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage covers a specific scenario: if a son inherits the right to qiṣāṣ against his father (e.g., the father killed the son's mother, and the son is the only heir), the son cannot execute qiṣāṣ against his father. The same rule applies if there are multiple heirs; if one heir is the son, the right drops entirely. The text provides two pieces of evidence: (1) the hadith 'a father is not killed for his child', and (2) the principle that if any heir forgives or if the right drops for some, it drops for all, based on the hadith about restraining and the precedent of Umar. This ensures that the father's status as a parent prevents execution, and the son's share of qiṣāṣ is negated due to the prohibition.

Key Terms
ورث قصاصا (waritha qiṣāṣan): To inherit the right to demand qiṣāṣ as an heir of the victim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The son must forgive the father.

Key Takeaway
A son cannot demand qiṣāṣ against his father; the right drops, and diya becomes the remedy.

Qiṣāṣ by Sword Only: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
States Hanafi ruling and refutes Shafi'i on qiṣāṣ method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that qiṣāṣ is executed only with the sword and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument based on equivalence.

Core Idea
Qiṣāṣ is only by sword – the explicit hadith overrides the principle of equivalence.

This passage presents the distinct Hanafi position that retaliatory execution (qiṣāṣ) must be carried out solely with a sword, regardless of the method used by the killer. It then refutes the Shafi'i school's argument for equivalence (mumathala) – that the killer should be executed in the same manner he killed. The dispute centers on the interpretation of prophetic hadith and the meaning of justice in retaliation.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: qiṣāṣ is executed only by sword, not by mimicking the killing method.
The Hanafi school holds this as a fixed rule.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i argues for equivalence: the killer should be killed in the same way he killed.
This is based on the Qur'anic principle of 'life for life' and a hadith.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the Shafi'i evidence: the hadith of the Jew crushed with stones for killing a slave-girl with stones.
This seems to support equivalence.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school prioritizes the explicit hadith 'La qawada illa bi al-sayf' which restricts the method to the sword.
The Jew case is interpreted as a special circumstance or before this ruling.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: under Hanafi, killer is executed by sword regardless; under Shafi'i, executed in same manner (e.g., drowning for drowning).
This affects how the state carries out capital punishment for qiṣāṣ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يستوفي القصاص فكلوا من كسب اولادکم
قصاص نہیں لیا جائے گا مگر تلوار ہے۔ قاتل نے چاہے اعضاء کوٹکڑے ٹکڑے کر کے مارا ہو لیکن اس سے قصاص اس طرح نہیں لیا جائے گا بلکہ تلوار سے ایک مرتبہ مار کر قتل کر دیا جائے گا۔ حدیث میں ہے۔ عن ابى بكرة قال قال رسول الله الا الله لا قود الا بالسيف (و) (ابن ماجہ شریف، باب لا قو دالا بالسیف ص ۳۸۴ نمبر ۲۲۶۸ دار قطنی، کتاب الحدود والدیات ج ثالث ص ۸۴ نمبر ۳۱۴۹) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ قصاص تلوار سے لیا جائے گا۔ نا امام شافعی فرماتے ہیں کہ برابری کے لئے جس طرح قاتل نے قتل کیا ہے اسی طرح قصاص میں قتل کیا جائے گا۔ (۱) تا کہ مساوات اور برابری ہو جائے (۲) حدیث میں ہے کہ ایک باندی کو یہودی نے پتھر سے کچل کر مارا تھا تو حضور نے یہودی کو پتھر حاشیہ : (الف) تم اور تمہارا مال تمہارے والد کے لئے ہیں۔ تمہاری اولاد تمہاری اچھی کمائی ہے۔ اس لئے اولاد کی کمائی سے کھاؤ (ب) آپ نے فرمایا قتل ہونے والے روک دیتے ہیں پہلے دسرے والے کو اگر چہ عورت کیوں نہ ہو۔ یعنی عورت معاف کر دے تو سب کی جانب سے قتل معاف ہو جائے گا ( ج ) حضرت عمر کے پاس مقدمہ پیش ہوا کہ ایک آدمی نے ایک آدمی کو قتل کیا۔ پس مقتول کے اولیاء نے اس کو قتل کرنا چاہا تو مقتول کی بہن نے کہا وہ قاتل کی بیوی بھی تھی کہ میں نے اپنا حصہ شوہر کو معاف کیا تو حضرت عمر نے فرمایا کہ قاتل قتل سے آزاد ہو گیا ( د) آپ نے فرمایا قصاص نہ لیا جائے مگر تلوار سے۔
And qiṣāṣ is not carried out except with the sword? Actually the Arabic is garbled: 'And qiṣāṣ is not carried out... eat from the earnings of your children.' The Urdu says: Qiṣāṣ is not taken except with the sword. Even if the killer cut the limbs into pieces, qiṣāṣ will not be taken in that way; rather, he will be killed with one strike of the sword. Hadith: from Abu Bakra that the Messenger of Allah ﷺ said: 'There is no qiṣāṣ except with the sword.' (Ibn Majah, Daraqutni). Imam al-Shafi'i says that for the sake of equivalence, qiṣāṣ should be carried out in the same manner as the killing. (1) So that equality is achieved. (2) There is a hadith that a Jew crushed a slave-girl with a stone, so the Prophet ﷺ crushed the Jew with a stone. ... Footnote (the footnote text is from previous lessons).
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling that qiṣāṣ must be carried out only with a sword, regardless of how the victim was killed. Even if the killer mutilated the victim, the state's execution is a single sword stroke. The proof is the hadith 'La qawada illa bi al-sayf' (Ibn Majah, Daraqutni). The text then presents the opposing view of Imam al-Shafi'i, who argues for equivalence (mumathala): the killer should be killed in the same way he killed. The Shafi'i argument is based on the Qur'anic principle of 'life for life' and the hadith of the Jew crushed with stones for killing a slave-girl with stones. The Hanafi response, implied by the citation of the sword hadith, is that the prophetic command for the sword overrides the general principle of equivalence. The Hanafi school prioritizes the specific, practical instruction over an abstract analogy. The footnote references support the Hanafi view.

Key Terms
قود (qawad): Qiṣāṣ, retaliatory execution.
قصاص (qiṣāṣ): Retaliatory punishment, usually execution for intentional killing.
مماثلة (mumathala): Equivalence; the principle that punishment should mirror the crime.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) In the same manner the victim was killed.
B) Only by the sword.
C) By the method chosen by the victim's heirs.
D) By burning if the victim was burned.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that qiṣāṣ must be by sword only, based on an explicit hadith, rejecting the Shafi'i principle of equivalence.

Qisas for Killing a Mukatab Without Wealth
Ruling on qisas when a mukatab is killed and leaves no wealth and only the master as heir

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a master can claim qisas for the intentional killing of his mukatab and identify the evidence from Quran and Sunnah.

Core Idea
Master gets qisas for mukatab only if no wealth and no other heir.

In this passage, we examine the Hanafi ruling on qisas (retaliatory justice) when a mukatab—a slave under contract of manumission—is intentionally killed. The central question is: who has the right to claim qisas when the mukatab leaves no other heir and insufficient wealth to complete his kitabah? The text clarifies that the master, as sole heir, can demand retribution, supported by prophetic precedent and Quranic command.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the intentional killing of a mukatab. The ruling is that the master has the right to claim qisas (retaliatory execution).
2
Locate the dalil
The passage cites a hadith where the Prophet killed a Jew for murdering a girl with stones, and Quran 16:126 commanding equal retaliation.
The hadith is from Bukhari; the Quranic verse is Surah Nahl 16:126.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith demonstrates the principle of qisas by action, and the Quranic verse explicitly commands retaliation in kind.
The Prophet's act shows implementation; the verse gives the rule.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Other schools may not consider a slave's life subject to qisas, but the Hanafi school affirms it under these specific conditions.
This is a mukhtalaf fih issue.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that qisas is wajib for the master provided the mukatab has no other heir and no wealth to complete kitabah.
This is from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Commentary
This passage states the Hanafi ruling on qisas when a mukatab (a slave under contract of manumission) is intentionally killed. If the mukatab leaves no wealth to complete his kitabah and has no heir except his master, then the master can claim qisas. The reasoning: since he dies as a slave and the master is the sole heir, there is no doubt (shubha) about the right to qisas. The commentator cites a hadith where the Prophet killed a Jew in retaliation for killing a girl with a stone, and the Quranic verse (16:126) prescribing equal retaliation. This establishes the principle of qisas for intentional killing.

Key Terms

قصاص (qisas): Retaliatory punishment for intentional killing or wounding, where the punishment matches the crime.

Qisas — Retaliation
intermediate

No Qisas for Killing Mukatab Who Leaves Wealth
Ruling with rational proof (shubha).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a mukatab is killed and leaves sufficient wealth with heirs other than the master, and explain why qisas is replaced by diyah due to shubha.

Core Idea
Shubha in ownership of the right to qisas drops qisas and makes diyah wajib.

We saw earlier that if a mukatab is killed and leaves no wealth, the master can claim qisas. But what if the mukatab leaves enough wealth to pay his kitabah and has other heirs besides the master? A conflict arises: does the right to qisas belong to the master or the other heirs? This lesson resolves that conflict by invoking the principle of shubha (legal uncertainty), which drops qisas entirely.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the act
A mukatab (a slave in a contract of kitabah) is intentionally killed. He leaves sufficient wealth to pay his kitabah and has at least one heir other than his master.
This is the second of two scenarios regarding a killed mukatab.
2
State the ruling
Neither the master nor the other heirs can claim qisas, even if they join together in demanding it. Qisas is void.
The Arabic says 'la qisas lahum' – no qisas for them.
3
Identify the proof (dalil aqli)
The ruling is based on rational reasoning: there is legal uncertainty (shubha) as to who has the right to claim qisas. The master could claim ownership of the slave, but the other heirs have a potential claim because the mukatab is on the verge of freedom and has wealth.
Shubha is a well-established principle in Hanafi fiqh that drops fixed punishments.
4
Explain the consequence
Because qisas is void due to shubha, diyah (blood money) becomes wajib (obligatory) on the killer.
Diyah is paid to the master and the other heirs according to their shares.
5
Compare with the first scenario
If the mukatab left no wealth, only the master can claim qisas. The presence of wealth and other heirs creates the shubha that drops qisas.
This distinction is crucial for correct application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۲۳۱۲) (۱۹) وان ترک وفاء ووارثه غير المولى فلا قصاص لهم وان اجتمعوا مع المولى 
(۲۳۱۲) (۱۹) اور اگر مال کتابت ادا کرنے کے لئے مال چھوڑا اور اس کے وارث آقا کے علاوہ ہے تو ان کے لئے قصاص کا حق نہیں ہے اگر چہ وہ آقا کے ساتھ مل کر مطالبہ کریں۔ مکاتب کو کسی نے جان بوجھ کر قتل کیا۔ اور مکاتب کی حالت یہ تھی کہ مال کتابت ادا کرنے کے لئے پورا مال چھوڑا تھا۔ اور آقا کے علاوہ دوسرے لوگ ان کے ورثہ موجود تھے اس صورت میں نہ آقا قصاص لے سکے گا اور نہ ورثہ قصاص لے سکیں گے۔ بلکہ قصاص ساقط ہو کر دیت لازم ہوگی۔
مکاتب چونکہ مال کتابت چھوڑ کر مر رہا ہے اور دوسرے لوگ وارث ہیں تو یہ کہا جاسکتا ہے کہ ایک قسم کا آزاد ہو کر مرا ہے۔ اور اس وقت آقا قصاص لینے کا حقدار ہے کیونکہ مال کتابت ادا نہیں کیا ہے۔ اس لئے شبہ ہو گیا کہ قصاص کون وصول کرے آقا یا وارث ۔ اور شبہ سے قصاص ساقط ہو جاتا ہے۔ اس لئے پہلے قصاص ساقط ہو جائے گا اور دیت لازم ہوگی۔
And if he (the mukatab) leaves wealth (to complete kitabah) and his heir is other than the master, then there is no qisas for them even if they join with the master.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage explains the second scenario for a killed mukatab. If the mukatab leaves sufficient wealth to pay his kitabah and has heirs besides the master, then neither the master nor the other heirs can claim qisas. The reason is uncertainty (shubha) as to who has the right: the master (because the slave belongs to him) or the other heirs (because the mukatab is on the verge of freedom). Because of this doubt, qisas is dropped and diyah (blood money) becomes obligatory. The commentary highlights that qisas cannot be applied when there is any uncertainty.

Key Terms
دية (diyah): Blood money or compensation paid to the heirs of the victim in lieu of qisas.
شبهة (shubha): Legal uncertainty that prevents enforcement of hudud and qisas punishments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The master can claim qisas alone.
B) The other heirs can claim qisas.
C) Both master and heirs together can claim qisas if they agree.
D) Qisas is dropped and diyah is paid.

Key Takeaway
When shubha exists in the right to claim qisas, qisas is void and diyah becomes obligatory.

Qisas — Retaliation
intermediate
6 min
Condition for Qisas on a Mortgaged Slave: Joint Demand by Mortgagor and Mortgagee
Ruling that qisas requires joint demand from both mortgagor and mortgagee.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for qisas on a rahn slave and explain why the mortgagee's consent is required.

Core Idea
Qisas on a mortgaged slave requires both owner and creditor to demand it, as the creditor's security interest is at stake.

When a slave held as collateral (rahn) is intentionally killed, the question arises: who can demand retribution? The Hanafi school establishes a unique condition that protects the rights of both the owner and the creditor. Understanding this ruling reveals how Shariah balances competing interests in joint property or collateral arrangements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and default ruling
The act is qisas (retaliatory killing) for intentional murder of a slave. Default: qisas is due from the killer, but here the slave is mortgaged, so ownership and possession are split.
Ownership remains with rahin, possession is with murtahin.
2
State the condition for qisas to become wajib
Qisas is not wajib until both rahin and murtahin jointly demand it. The word 'حتى يجتمع الراهن والمرتهن' means 'until they come together.'
Condition: both agree explicitly or implicitly.
3
Understand the reasoning behind the condition
If only rahin demands qisas, the slave is killed, and murtahin loses his security (the slave's value). If only murtahin demands, he cannot because he is not the owner. Both have rights: rahin's ownership and murtahin's lien.
Diyah (blood money) would compensate both, so if murtahin agrees to waive his right to diyah, rahin may take qisas alone.
4
Note the practical outcome
If they do not agree, diyah is taken and distributed: rahin gets ownership portion, murtahin gets his security amount. If they agree on qisas, it proceeds.
The Hanafi position safeguards both interests.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۳۱۳] (۲۰) واذا قتل عبد الرهن لا يجب القصاص حتى يجتمع الراهن والمرتهن 
[ ۲۳۱۳] (۲۰) واذا قتل عبد الرهن لا يجب القصاص حتى يجتمع الراهن والمرتهن 
مثلا زيد غریب راہن کا غلام عمر مالدار مرتضن کے پاس تھا۔ اس درمیان غلام قتل عمد میں مارا گیا جس کی وجہ سے اس کا قصاص لینا تھا تو را ہن اور مرتہن دونوں جمع ہو جائیں تو قصاص لیا جائے گاور نہ نہیں۔
مرتبہن تو اس لئے قصاص نہیں لے سکتا کہ اس کا غلام نہیں ہے غلام تو راہن کا ہے۔ اور راہن تنہا قصاص نہیں لے سکتا کہ اس سے مرتہن کا حق ضائع ہو گا ۔ پھر اس کا کچھ زور نہیں رہے گا ۔ کیونکہ اگر دیت لی جائے تو مرتہن کو بھی کچھ ملے گی اس لئے مرتہن اس بات پر راضی ہو کہ میں اپنا حق ساقط کرتا ہوں آپ قصاص لے لیں تب را من قصاص لے سکتا ہے۔ اس لئے راہن اور مرتہن دونوں کا جمع ہونا ضروری ہے۔
And if a slave held in mortgage (rahn) is killed, qisas is not obligatory until the mortgagor (rahin) and the mortgagee (murtahin) come together (to demand it).
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage discusses qisas for killing a slave that has been pledged as collateral (rahn). The ruling is that qisas cannot be carried out unless both the rahin (the one who pawned the slave) and the murtahin (the creditor who holds the slave as collateral) jointly demand it. The reason: if only the rahin demands qisas, the murtahin's right to the slave's value (as security) is lost because qisas destroys the slave, whereas diyah would compensate both parties. Therefore, the murtahin must consent to waive his claim so that rahin can take qisas. If only the murtahin demands, he cannot because he does not own the slave. Thus, both must agree.

Key Terms
رهن (rahn): A mortgage or pledge; a slave held as collateral for a debt.
راهن (rahin): The person who pledges the collateral.
مرتهن (murtahin): The creditor who holds the collateral.
قصاص (qisas): Retaliatory punishment: the killer is killed if the heirs demand it.
دية (diyah): Blood money paid to the heirs of the victim as compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling, when can qisas be carried out for killing a mortgaged slave?
A) Whenever the owner demands it.
B) Whenever the creditor demands it.
C) Only if both the owner and creditor demand it together.
D) It is never allowed; only diyah can be taken.

Key Takeaway
Qisas on a mortgaged slave requires joint demand by both the mortgagor and the mortgagee, safeguarding the mortgagee's security interest.

Qisas — Retaliation
intermediate

Qisas for Wounding Causing Death After Delay
Ruling that if a person intentionally wounds another and the victim remains bedridden until death, qisas applies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qisas when a wound leads to delayed death and identify the key condition (sahib firash) that determines the application of qisas.

Core Idea
If the victim never recovers and remains bedridden until death, qisas is wajib — even if death occurs long after the wound.

This passage addresses a common scenario: intentional wounding that does not kill instantly. The Hanafi school establishes a clear standard: the victim's continuous bedridden state (sahib firash) from wound to death is the decisive factor. The ruling is supported by hadith evidence and a rational principle to prevent gaps in justice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is intentional wounding that leads to death after a delay. The ruling: qisas is wajib.
2
Define the key condition
Understand 'sahib firash' (bedridden). The victim must remain confined to bed from the time of the wound until death.
The condition is continuous, not mere illness.
3
Locate the proof
The hadith of the Jew who killed a slave girl, and the report of Hasan al-Basri, establish that delayed death does not negate qisas.
These are ahad hadith, so the ruling is based on zanni evidence.
4
Analyze the rational argument
The commentator argues that if delay dropped qisas, many would escape punishment. The condition of recovery (not being bedridden) is the standard.
5
Apply the condition
If the victim recovered even temporarily, qisas is not due. If he never recovered, qisas applies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۳۱۴] (۲۱) ومن جرح رجلا عمدا فلم يزل صاحب فراش حتى مات فعليه القصاص 
(۲۳۱۴] (۲۱) کسی نے کسی آدمی کو جان بوجھ کر زخمی کیا اور وہ صاحب فراش رہا یہاں تک کہ مرگیا تو اس پر قصاص ہے۔ مثلا زید نے عمر کو اتنا زخمی کیا کہ وہ صاحب فراش ہو گیا ، چل پھر نہیں سکتا تھا۔ موت تک اسی حال میں رہا پھر مر گیا تو زید سے قصاص لیا جائے گا۔
زخمی کرنے کے بعد ٹھیک نہیں ہوا اسی حال میں مرگیا تو زخم ہی مرنے کا سبب بنا۔ اس لئے قصاص لیا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ اکثر ایسا ہوتا ہے کہ زخمی کرنے کے بعد فورا آدمی نہیں مرتا بلکہ کچھ دیر کے بعد مرتا ہے اس لئے اگر دیر ہونے سے قصاص ساقط کر دیں تو بہت سے قصاص ساقط ہو جائیں گے۔ اس لئے معیار یہ ہے کہ زخمی ہونے کے بعد صاحب فراش ہوا ہو اور اسی حال میں مرا ہو تو قصاص لیا جائے گا (۲) یہودی نے باندی کو پتھر سے زخمی کیا اور وہ دیر تک زندہ رہی اور صاحب فراش رہی اور اسی زخم سے انتقال کیا تو حضور نے یہودی سے قصاص لیا تھا۔ (بخاری شریف، باب من اقاد بالحجر ص ۲۰۱۶ نمبر ۶۸۷۹) (۲) عن الحسن في الرجل يضرب الرجل فلا يزال مضنى على فراشه حتى يموت قال فيه القود (الف) (مصنف ابن ابی شیبة ، ۱۳۵ الرجل يضرب الرجل فلا یزال مریضا حتی یموت ج خامس، ص ہیں ۴۲۲ نمبر ۲۷۶۱۸)
And whoever wounds a man intentionally and he continues to be bedridden until he dies, then qisas is upon him (the wounder).
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This ruling covers cases where intentional wounding does not cause immediate death. The standard is: if the victim remains bedridden (صاحب فراش) from the time of the wound until death, then qisas applies. The reasoning: death is attributed to the wound unless the victim recovered and later died from another cause. The commentator cites the hadith of the Jew who killed a girl with a stone; she was bedridden before death. Also, Hasan al-Basri reported that if a man strikes another and he remains sick on his bed until death, qisas is due.

Key Terms
صاحب فراش (sahib firash): A person confined to bed due to injury; used as a legal standard to attribute death to the wound.
قود (qawd): Another term for qisas, especially in the context of wounding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person intentionally wounds another, and the victim recovers from the wound but later dies from the same injury after a few months, does qisas apply?
A) Yes, because the wound caused the death.
B) No, because the victim recovered and then died later.
C) Yes, if the victim was bedridden the entire time.
D) No, because there is uncertainty.

Key Takeaway
Qisas for wounding that causes death after delay is wajib if and only if the victim remains bedridden (sahib firash) from the time of injury until death without recovery.

Qisas — Retaliation

Qisas for Cutting a Hand Intentionally at the Joint
Ruling with general principle for qisas on limbs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which qisas is applied to limbs according to the Hanafi school and identify which body parts are subject to qisas.

Core Idea
Qisas on limbs is only at the joint and for protruding, symmetrical parts.

This passage presents the Hanafi rule for retribution (qisas) when a limb is intentionally severed. The key principle is that qisas is only applied when the cut is made at a joint and the limb is a protruding, symmetrical body part. This ensures equality in retaliation, a core requirement of qisas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage rules on intentional cutting of a hand: if done at the joint, the aggressor's hand is cut similarly.
2
Extract the condition (joint)
The text specifies 'from the joint' (مفصل). This is the only permissible point for qisas on limbs.
3
List other body parts covered
The passage extends the same ruling to foot, soft part of nose (marim), and ear. Identify common feature: protruding, symmetrical, can be cut equally.
4
Derive the general principle
The rule: any body part that protrudes and can be cut equally is subject to qisas.
5
Note the limitation
If the cut is not at the joint, or part is not symmetrical, qisas is not applicable; instead, diyah (blood money) may be due.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۳۱۵](٢٢) ومن قطع يد رجل عمدا من 
[۲۳۱۵] (۲۲) کسی نے ہاتھ کاٹا جوڑ سے جان بوجھ کر تو اس کا هاتھ کا ٹا جائے گا ۔ ایسے ہی پاؤں اور ناک کا نرمہ اور کان ۔
قاعدہ یہ ہے کہ جو اعضاء جسم سے باہر ہو اور برابر سرابر کاٹا جا سکتا ہو قصاص میں اس کو کاٹا جائے گا۔ جیسے ہاتھ جوڑ سے کاٹا ہو تو قاتل کا
And whoever cuts the hand of a man intentionally from (the joint), then his hand should be cut (in retribution).
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage states that for intentional cutting of a limb, the same limb is cut in qisas, but only from a joint and if it is a symmetrical body part. The rule: limbs that protrude from the body and can be cut equally may be subject to qisas. Examples: hand at the wrist joint, foot at the ankle, the soft part of the nose (marim), and ear. The commentary stresses that the cut must be at the joint to ensure equal retaliation, as cutting elsewhere may not match.

Key Terms
مفصل (mufsal): Joint, the point at which a limb is attached to the body, used as the point of cutting in qisas.
مرمہ (marim): The soft cartilaginous part of the nose; mentioned as an example of a protruding organ subject to qisas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the general principle for qisas on limbs?
A) Any intentional cut on a limb results in qisas on the corresponding limb.
B) Only cuts at joints of protruding, symmetrical body parts result in qisas.
C) Cuts on limbs never result in qisas, only diyah.
D) Qisas for limbs is only applicable if the victim dies.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school applies qisas on limbs only when the cut is at a joint of a protruding, symmetrical body part, ensuring precise equality in retribution.

Qisas — Retaliation

Qisas for Gouging the Eye
Ruling on eye gouging: no qisas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that qisas does not apply to gouging an eye and explain why diyah is the penalty.

Core Idea
Gouging an eye cannot be exactly replicated, so qisas is impossible; diyah replaces it.

This lesson covers the first of two rulings on eye injuries in Hanafi fiqh. When an eye is completely extracted from its socket, exact equivalence cannot be achieved. Hence, the law prescribes blood money (diyah) rather than retributive justice (qisas).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
The act is striking someone's eye and gouging it out completely (qal').
Gouging means the eye is removed from its socket.
2
State the ruling
There is no qisas upon the offender; instead diyah (blood money) is wajib.
The ruling is derived from the impossibility of exact equivalence.
3
Understand the reason
Exact equivalence in gouging cannot be achieved due to variation in force, angle, and anatomy.
Qisas requires precise mumathala (equivalence).
4
Identify the evidence
The Quranic verse 'an eye for an eye' is interpreted to apply only when equivalence is possible. The practice of the Companions supports this distinction.
The verse is general, but the Hanafi school restricts it based on feasibility.

Source Text

[۲۳۱۷] (٢٤) فان كانت قائمة وذهب ضوء ها فعليه القصاص

المفصل جوڑ، مارن : ناک کا نرم حصہ ، نرمہ۔
[٢٢٣١٦ (٢٣) کسی نے کسی آدمی کی آنکھ پر مارا اور اس کو نکال ڈالا تو اس پر قصاص نہیں ہے۔

Al-Sharh al-Thamari, volume 3, page 300. Book of Jinayat. The joint: if his hand is cut, likewise the foot, and the soft part of the nose, and the ear. (23) Whoever strikes a man's eye and gouges it out, there is no qisas upon him. (24) If it remains and its sight goes, then qisas is upon him. The verse says: 'And We prescribed for them therein that a life for a life, an eye for an eye, a nose for a nose, an ear for an ear, a tooth for a tooth, and wounds are qisas' (Surah al-Ma'idah 5:45). From this verse it is understood that qisas is obligatory for the ear, eye, tooth, etc. (2) From Anas that the daughter of al-Nadr slapped a slave girl and broke her front tooth, so they came to the Prophet (ﷺ) and he ordered qisas. (Bukhari, Bab al-Sin bi al-Sin, p. 1018 no. 6894; Abu Dawud, Bab al-Qisas min al-Sin, p. 282 no. 2590). From this hadith it is understood that if one breaks a tooth, his tooth is broken in return. Al-Mafsalah: joint; Marin: the soft part of the nose, soft. [22316] (23) Someone struck a man's eye and extracted it, so there is no qisas upon him. Equality cannot be achieved in extracting another's eye, as there is excess or deficiency. Therefore no qisas, rather diyah is obligatory because the eye is not external but internal. And in the verse 'an eye for an eye', its meaning is that its vision is gone. And if vision can be taken in exchange for vision, then there is qisas for the eye. [2317] (24) And if the eye remains and its vision is gone, then qisas is upon him, such that a glass is heated for it and a wet loaf is placed on the face and the glass is brought before the eye until its vision goes. There is an athar from al-Hakam ibn 'Utaybah: A man slapped another or struck him otherwise, but he lost his eyesight while his eye remained. They wanted to execute qisas but became confused—they and the people—how to execute it. They did not know what to do. So they came to 'Ali (ﷺ), and he ordered that a coarse cloth be placed on his face, then he faced the sun and brought a mirror close to his eye, so his vision left while his eye remained. (Mussanaf 'Abd al-Razzaq, Bab... Footnote: (a) We prescribed for the Jews in the Torah: a life for a life... (b) Anas said: the daughter of al-Nadr slapped a girl... (a) al-Hakam ibn 'Utaybah said: a man slapped another...
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage presents two distinct rulings regarding qisas for eye injuries, supported by Quran, hadith, and practice of the Companions. The first ruling (no. 23) states that if a person strikes another's eye and gouges it out (yaf'aluha), there is no qisas. The reason, as explained in the Urdu commentary, is that exact equivalence is impossible—gauging an eye cannot be precisely replicated—so instead diyah (blood money) is obligatory. The second ruling (no. 24) applies when the eye remains in its socket but vision is lost: here qisas is required. The method of execution involves heating a mirror and placing it near the victim's eye under controlled conditions to destroy the sight, as demonstrated by 'Ali ibn Abi Talib (may Allah be pleased with him). The passage cites the Quranic verse 'an eye for an eye' as the primary evidence, interpreting it to mean that when the eye's function (vision) is lost, qisas applies because exact equivalence is achievable in extinguishing vision.

Key Terms
قلع العين (qal' al-'ayn): Extracting the eye from its socket completely.
قصاص (qisas): Retributive justice requiring exact equivalence in injury.
دية (diyah): Blood money paid as compensation for injury when qisas is not possible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if someone gouges out another's eye?
A) Qisas is wajib.
B) Diyah is wajib.
C) The victim chooses between qisas and diyah.
D) No penalty if accidental.

Key Takeaway
No qisas for eye gouging because exact equivalence is impossible; diyah is the penalty.

Qisas — Retaliation

Qisas for Loss of Sight
Ruling on loss of sight: qisas applies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that qisas applies when an eye remains but its vision is lost, and describe the method of execution.

Core Idea
Loss of sight can be exactly repaid by extinguishing the offender's vision, so qisas is wajib.

This lesson covers the second ruling on eye injuries: when the eye physically remains but sight is lost. Here, exact equivalence is achievable because the offender's vision can be eliminated in a controlled manner. Thus, qisas becomes wajib, as demonstrated by the practice of Caliph 'Ali.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
The act causes loss of vision while the eye remains intact (dhahab al-daw').
The eye is qa'imah (standing), meaning physically present.
2
State the ruling
Qisas is wajib upon the offender.
This is based on the Quranic verse and the method used by 'Ali.
3
Understand the method
The method involves heating a glass or mirror and placing it near the offender's eye to destroy sight without removing the eye.
This ensures precise equivalence: the offender loses vision but the eye remains.
4
Identify the evidence
Quran verse 'an eye for an eye' interpreted for loss of sight; athar from al-Hakam ibn 'Utaybah narrating 'Ali's practice.
The verse is applied literally when equivalence is possible.

(23) Whoever strikes a man's eye and gouges it out, there is no qisas upon him. (24) If it remains and its sight goes, then qisas is upon him. Al-Mafsalah: joint; Marin: the soft part of the nose, soft. [22316] (23) Someone struck a man's eye and extracted it, so there is no qisas upon him. Equality cannot be achieved in extracting another's eye, as there is excess or deficiency. Therefore no qisas, rather diyah is obligatory because the eye is not external but internal. And in the verse 'an eye for an eye', its meaning is that its vision is gone. And if vision can be taken in exchange for vision, then there is qisas for the eye. [2317] (24) And if the eye remains and its vision is gone, then qisas is upon him, such that a glass is heated for it and a wet loaf is placed on the face and the glass is brought before the eye until its vision goes. There is an athar from al-Hakam ibn 'Utaybah: A man slapped another or struck him otherwise, but he lost his eyesight while his eye remained. They wanted to execute qisas but became confused—they and the people—how to execute it. They did not know what to do. So they came to 'Ali (ﷺ), and he ordered that a coarse cloth be placed on his face, then he faced the sun and brought a mirror close to his eye, so his vision left while his eye remained. Commentary
This passage presents two distinct rulings regarding qisas for eye injuries, supported by Quran, hadith, and practice of the Companions. The first ruling (no. 23) states that if a person strikes another's eye and gouges it out (yaf'aluha), there is no qisas. The reason, as explained in the Urdu commentary, is that exact equivalence is impossible—gauging an eye cannot be precisely replicated—so instead diyah (blood money) is obligatory. The second ruling (no. 24) applies when the eye remains in its socket but vision is lost: here qisas is required. The method of execution involves heating a mirror and placing it near the victim's eye under controlled conditions to destroy the sight, as demonstrated by 'Ali ibn Abi Talib (may Allah be pleased with him). The passage cites the Quranic verse 'an eye for an eye' as the primary evidence, interpreting it to mean that when the eye's function (vision) is lost, qisas applies because exact equivalence is achievable in extinguishing vision.

Key Terms
ذهاب الضوء (dhahab al-daw'): Loss of vision while the eye physically remains.
قصاص (qisas): Retributive justice requiring exact equivalence in injury.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The victim may choose between qisas and diyah.
D) The offender must pay both diyah and undergo qisas.

Key Takeaway
Loss of sight with eye intact triggers qisas, as vision can be exactly reciprocated.

Qisas — Retaliation
beginner
6 min
Qisas for Teeth and Wounds; No Qisas for Bones; No Shibh 'Amd Below Life
Rulings on qisas for teeth and wounds; restriction for bones; no shibh 'amd below life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi rulings on qisas for teeth, wounds, and bones, and identify the textual proofs for each.

Core Idea
Qisas applies only where exact equivalence (mumathala) is possible; teeth are an external bone exception; otherwise diyah or hukumat 'adl.

This passage from al-Sharh al-Thamari presents core rulings on qisas (retaliation) for injuries. You will learn which body parts and wounds qualify for qisas, the role of exact equivalence, and why teeth are treated differently from other bones. The passage also clarifies that semi-intentional injury (shibh 'amd) does not apply below homicide, affecting how liability is classified.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three rulings
Read the matn carefully. It states three distinct rules: (1) Qisas is applicable to teeth. (2) Qisas is applicable to every wound where exact equivalence (mumathala) is possible. (3) No qisas for bones except the tooth. Additionally, there is a fourth point: below homicide there is no shibh 'amd.
These rulings are axiomatic in Hanafi fiqh.
2
Connect each ruling to its proof
For teeth: Quran 5:45 'the tooth for the tooth' and the hadith of Anas (Bukhari). For wounds: same verse 'and wounds are qisas'. For no qisas on bones: athar of 'Umar 'I will not enforce qisas for bones'. For no shibh 'amd below life: explicit statement of Imam Muhammad and Abu Hanifah.
The proofs are dalil naqli (textual).
3
Understand the principle of mumathala
Mumathala means exact equivalence in type, location, and extent of injury. Qisas is only wajib when such precise equivalence can be achieved. If not, the ruling shifts to diyah or hukumat 'adl.
4
Note the exception for teeth
Teeth are bones but external, so they can be broken exactly. Other bones are internal, making precise equivalence impossible. Hence, the general rule 'no qisas for bones' is specified by exception for teeth.
5
Recognize the absence of shibh 'amd below life
In Hanafi law, semi-intentional (shibh 'amd) is only relevant for homicide. For injuries below taking life, the only categories are intentional ('amd) or accidental (khata'), each carrying different consequences for qisas and diyah.

(25) And in the tooth there is qisas, and in every wound in which equality is possible there is qisas, and there is no qisas in bone except in the tooth. And its method is that wet cotton is placed on the other eye and a heated glass is brought before the first eye such that its vision leaves and qisas is exacted. (25) And qisas is applicable to the tooth, and to every wound where equality is possible; no qisas on bone except the tooth. For the tooth, the verse 'a tooth for a tooth and wounds are qisas' (45:5) has already passed that if he breaks a tooth, qisas is taken. (2) Hadith from Anas that the daughter of al-Nadr slapped a girl and broke her front tooth, so they came to the Prophet (ﷺ) and he ordered qisas. (Bukhari, Bab al-Sin bi al-Sin, p. 1018 no. 6894). (3) The tooth is a bone that is external to the body, so equivalence can be achieved, thus qisas can be taken. Other bones are not external, so they cannot be broken exactly equally, thus no qisas but diyah. For this there is an athar from 'Umar ibn al-Khattab: 'I will not enforce qisas for bones.' (Sunan al-Kubra, Bab la qisas fihi, vol. 8 p. 113 no. 16097). From this athar it is understood that there is no qisas for bones. However, due to the verse and hadith, qisas applies to the tooth. And for other wounds where exact equivalence is possible, qisas applies; the proof is the verse 'and wounds are qisas'. From Ibrahim: He said regarding samhaq, badi'ah, and similar wounds, whether accidental or intentional, if qisas cannot be executed, then there is a hukumat 'adl. Muhammad said: and we adopt this, and it is the saying of Abu Hanifah. (Kitab al-Athar li Imam Muhammad, Bab Diyat al-Asnan... p. 122 no. 562). From this it is also understood that where qisas is not possible, diyah is given. (26) There is no shibh 'amd below life; it is only 'amd or khata'. (a) Anas said... (b) 'Umar said... (c) Ibrahim said regarding samhaq and badi'ah...
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage covers three distinct yet connected rulings: (1) Qisas for teeth and wounds where exact equivalence (mumathala) is possible; (2) No qisas for bones except teeth; (3) The absence of shibh 'amd (semi-intentional homicide) below the level of taking life. The primary proof is the Quranic verse 'a tooth for a tooth and wounds are qisas' and the hadith of the Prophet (ﷺ) ordering qisas for a broken tooth. The Hanafi position, as transmitted by Imam Muhammad and Abu Hanifah, is that qisas is limited to cases where precise equivalence can be achieved. Teeth are an exception to the general rule of no qisas for bones because they are external and can be measured equally. For wounds where equivalence is not possible, such as those reaching the bone (samhaq) or flesh (badi'ah), the ruling is that of hukumat 'adl (assessment by a just arbitrator) rather than qisas. The final ruling (no. 26) clarifies that in injuries short of homicide, there is no category of shibh 'amd—only intentional ('amd) or accidental (khata')—which impacts the type of liability (qisas vs diyah).

Key Terms
مماثلة (mumathala): Exact equivalence in the type, location, and extent of injury.
شجة (shajjah): A head wound that reaches the bone.
شبه عمد (shibh 'amd): Semi-intentional: an act resembling intentional but with less intent.
حكومة عدل (hukumat 'adl): Assessment of compensation by a just arbitrator when fixed diyah is not prescribed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Qisas for injuries requires exact equivalence; teeth are the only bones eligible; otherwise compensation (diyah) or arbitrated assessment (hukumat 'adl) applies.

Qisas — Retaliation
intermediate
6 min
Qisas for Tooth and Wounds with Equivalence
States rules of qisas for teeth, wounds with equivalence, and bone exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on qisas for teeth and wounds, identify the exception for bones, and apply the condition of mumathalah (equivalence) to determine when qisas is permissible.

Core Idea
Qisas requires exact equivalence; applies to teeth and surface wounds, not to internal bones.

This passage from a Hanafi fiqh text lays down the foundational rule for qisas (retaliation) in cases of bodily injury. It specifies that qisas applies to teeth and to any wound where precise equivalence (mumathalah) can be achieved, but explicitly excludes all other bones. Understanding this rule requires careful attention to the condition of equivalence and the rationale behind the bone exception, which distinguishes the Hanafi school from some others.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what the passage is ruling upon. It states that qisas is established for the tooth (al-sinn) and for every wound (shajjah) where equivalence (mumathalah) is possible. Note that it also explicitly states there is no qisas for bone except the tooth.
The ruling is not merely a statement of permissibility but an assertion of obligation (wajib) when conditions are met.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The Quranic verse 'wal-sinn bil-sinn wal-juroohu qisas' (Surah al-Ma'idah 5:45) serves as the primary evidence. The phrase 'al-sinn bil-sinn' directly supports qisas for the tooth, while 'wal-juroohu qisas' is general and subject to the condition of mumathalah.
The verse is qat'i al-thubut (certain transmission) but zanni al-dalala (speculative meaning) regarding its scope.
3
Understand the condition of mumathalah
Qisas requires exact similarity in type, location, and extent of injury. For the tooth, equivalence is possible because each tooth has a unique position and form. For wounds, equivalence is possible only when the injury can be precisely matched.
This condition is derived from the principle of justice and from the practice of the Companions, particularly 'Umar.
4
Identify the exception for bones
The passage explicitly states 'la qisas fi 'azm illa fi al-sinn' — no qisas in bone except in the tooth. Internal bones cannot be exactly replicated, making mumathalah impossible. Therefore, for bone fractures (other than teeth), diyah (blood money) is paid.
The tooth is considered a bone but is external and easily matchable, hence the exception.
5
Evaluate the cross-madhhab disagreement
The Shafi'i school and others allow qisas for broken bones if precise matching is possible, arguing from the general verse. The Hanafi school restricts qisas to the tooth and wounds with equivalence, relying on the condition of mumathalah and the practice of 'Umar.
This difference stems from differing usul principles: Hanafis give weight to the condition of mumathalah as a necessary requirement for justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفى السن القصاص وفي كل شجة يمكن فيها المماثلة القصاص ولا قصاص فى عظم الا في السن
And in the tooth there is qisas, and in every wound in which equivalence is possible, there is qisas, and there is no qisas in bone except in the tooth.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on qisas (retaliation) for bodily injuries. It begins by stating that qisas is established for the tooth: if someone deliberately breaks another's tooth, the equivalent tooth of the offender may be broken in retaliation, provided it is similar in position and form. This is based on the Quranic verse 'al-sinn bil-sinn' (tooth for tooth) and the hadith of the Prophet ordering qisas for a broken tooth. Next, the passage extends the rule to every wound (shajjah) where precise equivalence (mumathalah) is possible. This means that for open wound- or head-wounds where the extent of damage can be matched with exact similarity, qisas is allowed. The general principle is that qisas requires exact reciprocity. Finally, the passage makes an exception: qisas is not permitted in bones except the tooth. The tooth is a bone that protrudes from the body and can be exactly matched, whereas other bones are internal and cannot be precisely equalised. For breaking other bones, the offender must pay diyah (blood money) or hukumat 'adl (fair arbitration). The Hanafi evidence includes the Quranic principle and the practice of 'Umar who said he would not enforce qisas for bones (other than teeth).

Key Terms
قصاص (qisas): Retaliation in kind for injury or homicide, restricted to cases where exact equivalence is possible.
شجة (shajjah): A wound on the head or face; in this context, any wound where qisas is applicable if equivalence possible.
مماثلة (mumathalah): Exact similarity in type, location, and extent of injury.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is qisas not allowed for breaking a bone such as the arm according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because bones are not covered by the Quranic verse
B) Because exact equivalence (mumathalah) is impossible for internal bones
C) Because only the tooth has a specific hadith
D) Because bones heal differently

Key Takeaway
Qisas for injury requires exact equivalence (mumathalah); therefore it applies to teeth and surface wounds that can be matched but not to internal bones, except the tooth.

Qisas — Retaliation
beginner
6 min
Shibh 'Amd Only in Soul, Not Below
Declares that shibh 'amd occurs only in homicide, not in injuries

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the scope of shibh 'amd in Hanafi fiqh and distinguish it from 'amd and khata' in injuries.

Core Idea
Shibh 'amd applies only to homicide, not to injuries below the soul.

In Hanafi criminal law, the category of shibh 'amd (quasi-intentional) occupies a middle ground between intentional and accidental homicide. This passage from Al-Sharh al-Sameri declares a crucial boundary: shibh 'amd does not exist for injuries short of death. Understanding this limitation prevents misapplication of the category to wounding cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? The passage states that shibh 'amd is limited to homicide (al-nafs) and does not extend to injuries below that (ma duna al-nafs).
2
Define shibh 'amd
Recall the Hanafi definition: a strike with a tool not normally lethal, where the striker intended to strike but not to kill, yet death results. This category requires diyah mughallazah.
Key condition: tool is non-lethal.
3
Apply the scope
Why does shibh 'amd not apply to injuries? For injuries, the same non-lethal tool can cause harm intentionally or accidentally; there is no doubt about intent to kill because death did not occur. Thus, injuries are either 'amd or khata'.
The doubt that creates shibh 'amd only exists when death occurs.
4
Note cross-madhhab difference
The Shafi'i school does not recognize shibh 'amd at all; they classify all cases of death from non-lethal tools as khata'. Understand the practical difference in diyah.
Hanafi: heavy diyah (mughallazah); Shafi'i: regular diyah (mukhaffafah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس فيما دون النفس شبه عمد
And there is no shibh 'amd in what is below the soul [i.e., injuries less than homicide].
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage is a concise ruling from the Hanafi school defining the scope of shibh 'amd (quasi-intentional homicide). It states that the category of shibh 'amd applies exclusively to homicide (mafs al-nafs) and does not apply to injuries short of death (ma duna al-nafs). In Hanafi fiqh, shibh 'amd is when a person intentionally strikes with a tool that is not normally lethal (e.g., a stick or stone), and the victim dies. This is treated as a middle category between 'amd (intentional) and khata' (accident), requiring diyah mughallazah (heavy blood money). However, for injuries such as cutting a hand or causing a wound, there are only two possibilities: either intentional ('amd) or accidental (khata'). The reason is that with non-lethal tools, one cannot claim the intent was only to injure; if injury occurs, it is either intentional or accidental. Shibh 'amd is specific to death because the tool's non-lethality creates doubt about the intent to kill. This ruling is based on the practice of the Companions, as narrated from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i.

Key Terms
شبه عمد (shibh 'amd): Quasi-intentional homicide: striking with a non-lethal tool causing death, resulting in heavy diyah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Shibh 'amd
B) 'Amd or khata'
C) Always khata'
D) Always 'amd

Key Takeaway
Shibh 'amd in Hanafi law is exclusively for homicide, not for injuries below the soul.

Qisas — Retaliation

No Qisas Between Man and Woman for Wounds Below Soul
States that qisas is not applied between genders for non-lethal injuries, with Shafi'i dissent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qisas for non-lethal injuries between a man and a woman, explain the Shafi'i objection, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
For non-lethal wounds, Hanafi rejects gender-equal qisas; only diyah is due.

This passage presents a foundational Hanafi distinction: qisas for homicide is gender-neutral, but for wounds below the soul, the school bars qisas between a man and a woman. Why? The Hanafi proof relies on Companion practice and the principle that equality in retaliation is impossible when the woman's diyah is half. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, citing the general 'wounds are qisas' verse. Understanding this ikhtilaf reveals how the Hanafi school prioritizes precise legal equality over general textual commands.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds that there is no qisas between a man and a woman for intentional wounds below homicide; the perpetrator must pay diyah.
2
Identify the opposing school and their evidence
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that both are humans with equal inviolability, citing the general Quranic verse 'wal-juroohu qisas' and a hadith where the Prophet ordered qisas for a woman wounding a man.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school restricts the general text with the practice of 'Umar and the principle that women's diyah is half; exact qisas would be unequal.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Shafi'i, a man's hand may be cut off for cutting a woman's hand; under Hanafi, only diyah is paid.
5
Identify the tarjih and its basis
The Hanafi position is the mufta bihi, supported by Companion practice and the need for exact proportionality in qisas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا قصاص بين الرجل والمرأة فيما دون النفس ولا بين الحر والعبد ولا بين العبدين
And there is no qisas between the man and the woman in what is below the soul, nor between the free and the slave, nor between two slaves.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on qisas for non-lethal injuries (ma duna al-nafs) when parties differ in gender or status. It states three rules: (1) No qisas between a man and a woman for wounds below homicide; (2) No qisas between a free person and a slave; (3) No qisas between two slaves. The commentary explains that this applies only to injuries short of death; if a man kills a woman, qisas is applicable. The Hanafi evidence includes an athar from Hammad that said there is no qisas between man and woman for intentional wounds below the soul. The passage then presents the opposing view of Imam al-Shafi'i, who argues that both are humans and thus have equal inviolability; he cites the general Quranic verse 'wal-juroohu qisas' (wounds are subject to qisas) without differentiation between male and female. He also cites a hadith where the Prophet ordered qisas for a woman who wounded a man. The Hanafi response is that these evidences are general but the Hanafi school restricts text with the practice of the Companions (e.g., 'Umar) and the principle that the woman's liability is half diyah, so qisas cannot be exactly equal. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is the Hanafi one: no qisas for wounds between man and woman; only diyah is due.

Key Terms
قصاص (qisas): Retaliation in kind; here, applied to wounds.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a woman cuts off a man's hand intentionally, what is the ruling?
A) Qisas on the woman's hand
B) Diyah
C) Ta'zir
D) No penalty

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school bars qisas for non-lethal wounds between a man and a woman, relying on Companion practice and the principle of exact proportionality.

Qisas — Retaliation

No Qisas Between Free and Slave or Two Slaves for Wounds
Ruling on qisas between free and slave, and between two slaves for limb injuries

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qisas for limb injuries between free and slave, and between two slaves, and identify the key proofs (hadith and athar) that support this ruling.

Core Idea
For limb wounds, there is no qisas between free and slave, nor between two slaves; only diyah is owed.

This lesson examines a specific area of qisas (retaliation) law: injuries between individuals of different social status (free vs slave) or between two slaves. The Hanafi school holds that qisas for limbs is restricted to cases where the parties are of equal status. We will explore the rulings and their proofs from the Sunnah and the practice of the Companions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage deals with intentional wounding of a slave by a free person, and wounding between two slaves. Identify the specific act (limb injury) and whether qisas or diyah applies.
For free-on-slave limb injury: no qisas, only diyah. For slave-on-slave limb injury: no qisas, only diyah, except for intentional killing.
2
Locate the proofs (dalil)
Two proofs are cited: a hadith from Ibn Abbas that a free person is not killed for a slave (used to argue a fortiori for limbs), and an athar from Ibn Mas'ud that a slave is not subjected to qisas for wounding another slave except in intentional killing.
These are naqli proofs (textual), not qiyas.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah (how the proof supports the ruling)
For free-slave: the hadith prohibits qisas for killing (soul). Since limbs are less severe (a fortiori), qisas for limbs is also not allowed. For slave-slave: the athar explicitly states no qisas for wounds.
The argument uses the principle of 'اولى' (a fortiori) for free-slave.
4
Note the ikhtilaf (scholarly disagreement)
Imam Abu Hanifah holds that a free person is killed in retribution for killing a slave (contrary to the Shafi'i view). However, for limbs, the schools agree (no qisas). The text mentions this distinction.
The cross-madhhab difference is limited to homicide, not limbs.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih (preferred position)
The mufta bihi position is that for limb injuries between free and slave or between two slaves, qisas is not applicable; diyah is wajib. This is based on the strong textual evidence.
Authority weight: zahir al-riwayah.

Chapter of qisas between men and women in wounds p. 1017 no. 6886) From this athar it is understood that for a woman's limbs, qisas will be taken from a man. If a free person intentionally cuts off or wounds the hand or foot of a slave, then qisas will not be taken from the free person; rather his diyah will be taken. However, if he kills intentionally, then according to Imam Abu Hanifah, qisas for the slave will be taken from the free person. And if a slave wounds another slave, there is no qisas; its proof is this athar: From Abdullah ibn Mas'ud: The slave is not subject to qisas from a slave for an intentional wound nor for an accidental wound except in intentional killing. Commentary
This passage addresses qisas between free and slave, and between two slaves. The Hanafi ruling is clear: for limb injuries, there is no qisas between a free person and a slave; the free person pays diyah. For intentional killing, Abu Hanifah holds that a free person can be subjected to qisas for killing a slave (unlike the Shafi'i view). For wounds between two slaves, qisas is also not applicable; only diyah. The proofs are from Ibn Abbas (hadith that a free is not killed for a slave) and from Ibn Mas'ud (athar that a slave is not subjected to qisas from another slave except for intentional killing). The reasoning is a fortiori: if qisas is not applied for killing a free, it is even less likely for limbs.

الحر (al-hurr): A free person (not a slave).
العبد (al-'abd): A slave.

Qisas — Retaliation

Qisas Between Muslim and Dhimmi for Limb Injuries
Ruling that qisas is obligatory between Muslim and dhimmi for limbs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qisas between a Muslim and a dhimmi for limb injuries, identify the dalil (the practice of Umar), and explain the wajh al-dalalah.

Core Idea
A Muslim who injures a dhimmi's limb is subject to qisas, established by Umar's binding precedent.

In Hanafi fiqh, the inviolability of a dhimmi's limbs is equal to that of a Muslim for the purposes of qisas. This lesson examines the ruling through a key incident from the Caliph Umar's practice, where he required a Muslim to submit to qisas for wounding a dhimmi. Understanding this shows how the school relies on the practice of the Companions (qawl sahabi) as a source of law.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the ruling and its scope
State clearly: qisas is wajib for limb injuries between a Muslim and a dhimmi. Note that this applies to intentional injuries.
2
2. Locate the primary evidence
The proof is the athar (report) of Makhul about Umar ibn al-Khattab. In this incident, Ubadah ibn al-Samit wounded a dhimmi, and Umar ordered him to submit to qisas (al-qawad).
This is classified as qawl sahabi with zanni thubut but qati dalalah.
3
3. Analyse the wajh al-dalalah
Umar's explicit demand for qisas establishes the principle that a Muslim is liable for retaliation for harming a dhimmi's limb. Zayd ibn Thabit's initial objection shows the issue was contested, but Umar's insistence settled the practice.
The dalalah is qati because the action directly enforces the ruling.
4
4. Consider the cross-madhhab ikhtilaf
Some schools (e.g., Shafi'i in one view) hold that qisas only applies between Muslims. They analogize from the rule that a Muslim is not killed for a disbeliever (la yuqtalu muslimun bi kafir). Hanafis distinguish by the strength of the Companion practice.
The thamarah al-khilaf: the Hanafi position allows qisas for limbs; the opposing view limits it to diyah.
5
5. Understand the practical consequence
If a Muslim injures a dhimmi's limb, the victim may demand qisas or accept diyah. The judge must enforce qisas if the victim insists, following Umar's precedent.
This elevates the dhimmi's bodily integrity to parity with a Muslim in criminal law.

Qisas — Retaliation
intermediate
12 min
Qisas in Limbs between Muslim and Non-Muslim, Free and Slave
Ruling with proofs from hadith and athar on qisas between different statuses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qisas for limbs between Muslim and dhimmi, and between free and slave, and explain the proofs and disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
Qisas applies to limbs between Muslim and dhimmi but not between free and slave; life is treated differently than limbs.

This passage tackles a critical area of Islamic criminal law: retaliation (qisas) for injuries to limbs. The Hanafi school distinguishes between categories of persons—Muslim versus non-Muslim, free versus slave—based on specific hadith and the practice of the Companions. Understanding these distinctions is essential for correctly applying qisas and avoiding confusion with the Shafi'i position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rulings stated
First, locate the three scenarios: (1) Muslim vs. dhimmi for limbs, (2) free vs. slave for limbs, (3) slave vs. slave for wounds. Note the ruling for each.
The passage opens with a general rule and then gives exceptions.
2
Examine the proofs for each
For Muslim-dhimmi: the athar of Umar ordering qisas for a head wound. For free-slave: the hadith 'A free man is not killed for a slave' applied by stronger reason (mafhum al-awla). For slave-slave: the athar of Ibn Mas'ud.
The author uses a fortiori reasoning for limbs from the life ruling.
3
Understand the Shafi'i objection
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that since a Muslim is not killed for a non-Muslim, limbs should follow the same rule. He also cites a narration where Umar ultimately ordered diya.
The dispute hinges on whether the hadith about life applies to limbs by analogy.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school distinguishes between life and limbs: the hadith about killing is specific to life, while the practice of Umar shows a different principle for limbs. The diya narration may be a later compromise.
The Hanafi position relies on the practice of Umar and the general principle of equality.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Hanafi, a Muslim who cuts a dhimmi's hand loses his own hand; under Shafi'i, he pays diya only.
This is the difference in applied criminal law.

الحر والعبد ولا بين العبدين (۲۸) ويجب القصاص فى الاطراف بین المسلم باب القصاص بين الرجال والنساء في الجراحات ص ۱۰۱۷ نمبر ۶۸۸۶) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ عورت کے اعضاء کے بدلے مرد سے قصاص لیا جائے گا۔ آزاد غلام کے ہاتھ پاؤں کو جان بوجھ کر کاٹ دے یا زخمی کر دے تو آزاد سے قصاص نہیں لیا جائے گا بلکہ اس کی دیت لی جائے گی۔ البتہ جان کر قتل کر دے تو امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک غلام کا قصاص آزاد سے لیا جائے جائے گا۔ اور غلام غلام کو زخمی کرے تو قصاص نہیں ہے اس کی دلیل یہ اثر ہے۔ عن عبد الله بن مسعود ان العبد لايقاد من العبد في جراحة عمد ولا خطاء الا في قتل عمد (ب) (مصنف ابن ابی شیبۃ ۷۳ العبد يجرح العبدج خامس، ص ۱۳۸۹ نمبر ۲۷۲۳۶) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ غلام غلام کو زخمی کر دے تو قصاص نہیں لیا جائے گا بلکہ دیست لازم ہوگی۔ [۲۳۲۱] (۲۸) قصاص واجب ہے اعضاء میں مسلم اور کافر کے درمیان۔ مسلمان کا فریعنی ذمی کے اعضاء کو جان بوجھ کر کاٹ دے تو مسلمان سے قصاص لیا جائے گا۔ اثر میں ہے۔ حدثنی مکحول قال لما قدم علينا عمر بيت المقدس اعطى عبادة بن الصامت رجلا من اهل الذمة دابته يمسكها فابي عليه فشجه موضحة ثم دخل المسجد فلما خرج عمر صاح النبطى الى عمر فقال عمر من صاحب هذا؟ قال عبادة انا صاحب هذا ما اردت الى هذا ؟ قال اعطيته دابتي يمسكها فابي وكنت امرء في حد قال اما ا لا فاقعد للقود فقال له زيد بن ثابت ما كنت The free man and the slave, and not between the two slaves. (28) Qisas is obligatory in limbs between the Muslim. Section on qisas between men and women in wounds p. 1017 no. 6886. From this athar it is known that a man is subject to qisas for the limbs of a woman. If a free man deliberately cuts off the hands or feet of a slave or wounds him, qisas will not be taken from the free man but rather diya will be taken. However, if he kills him intentionally, according to Imam Abu Hanifa qisas will be taken from the free man for the slave. Regarding life, Imam Abu Hanifa says the same as Imam al-Shafi'i: that the limbs of a free man are not cut for the limbs of a slave. The proof is the same hadith: from Ibn Abbas that the Prophet (ﷺ) said: 'A free man is not killed for a slave.' (a) (al-Daraqutni, Kitab al-Hudud wa al-Diyat, vol. 3, p. ...; al-Bayhaqi, Bab la yuqtal hurr bi'abd, vol. 3, p. 63, no. 15939) Since qisas for life is not taken from this hadith, then a fortiori qisas for limbs is not taken. And if a slave wounds a slave, there is no qisas; its proof is this athar: from Abdullah ibn Mas'ud: 'A slave is not subjected to qisas for a slave in intentional injury nor in error, except in intentional killing.' (b) (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, vol. 5, p. 1389, no. 27236) From this athar it is known that if a slave wounds a slave, qisas will not be taken but diya will be obligatory. [2321] (28) Qisas is obligatory in limbs between Muslim and non-Muslim. If a Muslim deliberately cuts the limbs of a dhimmi, qisas will be taken from the Muslim. There is an athar: Makhul narrated: When Umar came to Bayt al-Maqdis, 'Ubadah ibn al-Samit gave a dhimmi man his animal to hold, but he refused, so 'Ubadah struck him on the head causing a wound that exposed the bone. Then he entered the mosque. When Umar came out, the Nabatean shouted to Umar. Umar said: 'Who did this?' 'Ubadah said: 'I did it. I did not intend to hit him that hard. I gave him my animal to hold and he refused, and I was in a state of anger.' Umar said: 'In any case, sit for qisas.' Zayd ibn Thabit said to him: 'Would you take qisas from your brother for your slave?' Umar said: 'By Allah, if I forego qisas for you, I will help you with the diya. Give him double the blood money.' (c) (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, vol. 7, p. 446, no. ...) Footnote: (a) He (ﷺ) said: A free man is not killed for a slave. (b) Abdullah ibn Mas'ud said: In intentional wounding, a slave is not subjected to qisas from a slave, nor in accidental wounding, except in intentional killing. (c) Makhul said: When Umar came to Bayt al-Maqdis, 'Ubadah ibn al-Samit gave a dhimmi man his animal to hold, but he refused, so he struck him on the head and wounded him. Then he entered the mosque. When Umar came out, the Nabatean came shouting to Umar. Umar asked: Who did this? 'Ubadah said: I did it, but I did not intend to hit that hard. I gave him my animal to hold and he refused, and I was somewhat angry. Umar said: In any case, sit for qisas. Zayd ibn Thabit said: Would you take qisas from your brother for your slave? Umar said: If I keep away from qisas, I will help you with the diya. Give him double the blood money. Imam al-Shafi'i says: When a Muslim kills a non-Muslim, there is no qisas; so if he cuts his limbs, how can qisas be obligatory? Rather diya is obligatory. The hadith passed: 'A Muslim is not killed for a non-Muslim.' (Sahih al-Bukhari, Bab la tuqtal al-muslim bi al-kafir, p. 102, no. 7915) Moreover, in the athar of Umar in Sunan al-Bayhaqi, it is stated that diya is obligatory, not qisas: 'Umar abandoned qisas and judged diya upon him.' (a) (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, riwayat 'an Umar ibn al-Khattab, vol. 8, p. 32, no. 15926) From this it is known that qisas is not obligatory.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive ruling on qisas (retaliation) for injuries to limbs, distinguishing between different categories of persons: Muslim and non-Muslim, free and slave. The author begins by stating the general rule: qisas is wajib (obligatory) for limbs between a Muslim and a non-Muslim (meaning a dhimmi). He then clarifies that between a free man and a slave, qisas for limbs is not taken; only diya (blood money) is paid. However, for intentional killing, Imam Abu Hanifa holds that a free man may be killed for a slave. The proof for the limb ruling is the famous hadith: 'A free man is not killed for a slave,' which by stronger reason (mafhum al-awla) applies to limbs. Similarly, between two slaves, qisas for wounds is not taken except for intentional killing, as per the athar of Ibn Mas'ud. The passage then addresses the controversy with Imam al-Shafi'i: the Hanafi school holds that qisas applies to limbs between Muslim and dhimmi, relying on the practice of Umar (رضي الله عنه) who initially ordered qisas for a head wound inflicted by 'Ubadah on a dhimmi. The Shafi'i school argues that since a Muslim is not killed for a non-Muslim, limbs should follow the same rule, citing the hadith 'La yuqtalu muslimun bi kafir' and a different narration of Umar's incident where he ordered diya. The Hanafi response distinguishes between life and limbs, and argues that the hadith about life is specific, while the general principle of equality applies to limbs. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is the Hanafi view that qisas is wajib for limbs between Muslim and dhimmi.

Key Terms
قصاص (qisas): Retaliation in kind for intentional injury or killing, an exact equivalent.
دیت (diya): Blood money paid to the victim or heirs as compensation for injury or homicide when qisas is not applicable.
ذمی (dhimmi): A non-Muslim living under the protection of an Islamic state, paying jizya.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if a Muslim cuts off the hand of a dhimmi intentionally?
A) The Muslim's hand is cut in qisas.
B) Only diya is paid, no qisas.
C) The Muslim is executed.
D) No penalty because the victim is a non-Muslim.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school mandates qisas for limbs between Muslim and dhimmi, but not between free and slave (except for life), based on hadith and the practice of Umar.
