Qisas — Retaliation
intermediate
6 min
No Qisas When Injury Heals After Cutting or Deep Wound
Ruling with proof: no qisas when wound heals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that qisas is not applicable when an intentional wound heals without defect and explain the legal reasoning (impossibility of exact equivalence) with reference to the athar of Ibrahim al-Nakha'i.

Core Idea
Qisas requires exact equivalence; if the wound heals, equivalence is impossible, so diya is due.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi principle in jinayat: qisas (retaliation) is only valid when exact equivalence (mumathalah) is achievable. If an intentional wound, such as cutting a hand from halfway the forearm or a deep belly wound (ja'ifah), heals without defect, exact retaliation becomes impossible—cutting the offender's hand might not heal similarly, and a belly wound cannot be inflicted to heal identically. Therefore, the Sharia substitutes diya (blood money) for qisas. The matn ruling is supported by the athar of Ibrahim al-Nakha'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and the Ruling
Determine exactly what act is being judged: cutting a hand from half the forearm or inflicting a ja'ifah wound. The ruling: no qisas, and diya is obligatory.
2
Understand the Legal Reason (Illah)
The reason qisas is dropped is the impossibility of exact equivalence. Since the victim healed, cutting the offender's hand or wounding his belly would not be exactly equivalent because the outcome (healing) cannot be guaranteed.
Equality is a condition for qisas in the Hanafi school.
3
Locate the Dalil (Proof)
The matn is supported by the athar of Ibrahim al-Nakha'i: any intentional wound for which qisas is not possible obligates compensation from the injurer's own property. This is a Qawl Sahabi, zanni in transmission but accepted in the Hanafi school.
Reference: Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, no. 27404.
4
Note the Cross-Madhhab Mention
The commentary briefly contrasts Imam al-Shafi'i's position on Muslim-non-Muslim cases, but the main ruling is consistent across schools: when qisas is impossible, diya applies.
This is a passing reference; the lesson focus remains on the Hanafi view.
5
Determine the Practical Consequence (Thamarah)
In practice, if the offender cannot be subjected to qisas because the wound healed, he must pay diya (or arsh for broken bones that heal without defect). This ensures the victim receives compensation without violating the principle of equivalence.
Example: Zayd cuts Umar's hand; hand heals; Zayd pays full diya for a hand.

لازم ہوگی۔ خطرناک انداز میں ہاتھ کٹنے کے بعد ٹھیک ہونا ضروری نہیں ہے آدمی اس سے مر بھی سکتا ہے۔ اب اس قسم کا قصاص کہ عمر کا پہنچے سے ہاتھ کاٹے پھر وہ ٹھیک بھی ہو جائے یہ ممکن نہیں ہے اور قصاص میں برأت ولم ينقص منها شيء ارشها مائة وثمانون درهما ( ج ) ( مصنف ابن ابی هیته ۵۴ الید او الرجل تگسر ثم تیر آج خامس ص ۳۷۸، نمبر ۲۷۱۰۱) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ ٹھیک ہونے کے بعد دیت لازم ہو گی قصاص لازم نہیں ہوگا۔ یں جس زخم کا برابر سرا بر قصاص لینا نا ممکن ہو اس کی دیت لازم ہوگی قصاص نہیں۔ انت الجائعة : پیٹ کے اندر پہنچا ہوا زخم ۔ حاشیہ : (الف) حضرت عمر نے قصاص چھوڑا اور دیت کا فیصلہ کیا (ب) حضرت ابراہیم نے فرمایا جان بوجھ کر کیا ہوا زخم جن کا قصاص نہیں لیا جا سکتا ہواس کا تاوان زخم کرنے والے پر ہے (ج) حضرت ابراہیم نے فرمایا جب ہاتھ یا پاؤں ٹوٹ جائے پھر ٹھیک ہو جائے اور اس میں کچھ کمی نہ ہوتو اس کی ارش ایک سواسی درہم ہے۔
[2322] (29) Whoever cuts the hand of a man from half the forearm, or wounds him with a penetrating (ja'ifah) wound, and then he recovers from it, then there is no qisas upon him. 27860) From this athar it is known that if a non-Muslim's limb is cut, qisas will be taken from the Muslim. According to Imam al-Shafi'i, when a non-Muslim's life is killed, there is no qisas; so if he cuts his limbs, how can qisas be obligatory? Rather diya is obligatory. The hadith passed: 'A Muslim is not killed for a non-Muslim.' (Sahih al-Bukhari, Bab la tuqtal al-muslim bi al-kafir, p. 102, no. 7915) Moreover, in the athar of Umar in Sunan al-Bayhaqi, it is stated that diya is obligatory, not qisas: 'Umar abandoned qisas and judged diya upon him.' (a) From this it is known that qisas is not obligatory. [2322] (29) Someone cut someone's hand from halfway, or inflicted a deep wound in the belly, then he recovered from it, then there is no qisas upon him. For example, Zayd cut 'Umar's hand from the wrist, then it healed; then Zayd's hand will not be cut in qisas, but rather his diya is obligatory. Similarly, Zayd inflicted a deep wound in 'Umar's belly, then the wound healed; then as qisas, a deep wound will not be inflicted in Zayd's belly, but rather his diya is obligatory. After a hand is cut in a dangerous manner, it is not necessary that it heals; a person may die from it. Now this kind of qisas—that 'Umar's hand is cut from the wrist and then it also heals—is impossible, and equality is necessary in qisas; therefore qisas is not taken, diya is obligatory. The same applies to a deep belly wound: that the wound heals after occurrence, and likewise to take qisas from the injurer—that after inflicting a deep wound it also heals—is impossible. Therefore qisas is not taken from him, diya is obligatory. From Ibrahim: 'Any intentional wound for which qisas is not possible, its compensation is upon the injurer in his property, not upon his 'a'qila.' (b) (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, vol. 5, p. 403, no. 27404) From this athar it is known that for a wound from which qisas is impossible, its diya is taken. (2) There is an athar: From Ibrahim: 'It used to be said: When a hand or foot is broken, then it heals and nothing is missing from it, its arsh (compensation) is one hundred and eighty dirhams.' (c) (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, vol. 5, p. 378, no. 27101) From this athar it is known that after healing, diya is obligatory, not qisas. For any wound for which exact equivalent qisas is impossible, its diya is obligatory, not qisas. Al-ja'ifah: a wound that reaches the inside of the belly. Footnotes: (a) Umar abandoned qisas and decreed diya. (b) Ibrahim said: For an intentional wound for which qisas cannot be carried out, its compensation is upon the injurer from his own wealth, not upon his 'a'qila. (c) Ibrahim said: When a hand or foot is broken, then it heals and nothing is missing from it, its arsh is one hundred and eighty dirhams.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the matn: 'If someone cuts a hand from half the forearm, or inflicts a penetrating (ja'ifah) wound to the belly, and then the victim recovers, there is no qisas upon the offender.' The author explains the reasoning: qisas requires exact equivalence (mumathalah), but in these cases exact equivalence is impossible. For a hand cut from the wrist, if the victim's hand heals, cutting the offender's hand would not be equivalent because the offender's hand might not heal in the same way. Similarly, for a deep belly wound, it is impossible to inflict an identical wound that heals exactly. Therefore, only diya (blood money) is due. The author supports this with an athar from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i that any intentional wound for which qisas is not possible requires compensation from the offender's own wealth. Another athar states that if a broken bone heals without defect, the arsh (fixed compensation) is 180 dirhams. This clarifies that the ruling applies to any injury where exact retaliation is impossible. The passage also includes a brief interjection about the Shafi'i position on Muslim-non-Muslim cases, but the main focus is on impossibility of qisas due to healing. The key takeaway is that qisas is only valid when exact equivalence is possible; otherwise, monetary compensation (diya or arsh) substitutes.

Key Terms
جائفة (ja'ifah): A wound that penetrates into the internal cavity of the body (e.g., belly, chest).
ارش (arsh): Fixed monetary compensation for bodily injuries that do not have a prescribed diya amount, often proportional to the severity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person cuts another's hand from halfway the forearm and the wound heals completely, what is the ruling according to the Hanafi school?
A) No penalty because the victim healed.
B) The offender's hand is cut in qisas.
C) Diya (blood money) is obligatory.
D) The offender is imprisoned.

Key Takeaway
When an intentional wound heals without defect, qisas is impossible due to lack of exact equivalence, so diya (or arsh) becomes wajib.

Qisas — Retaliation

Qisas Not Required When Exact Retaliation Impossible; Muslim-Kafir Limb Injury
States ruling that no qisas for certain injuries; discusses Muslim-kafir qisas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which qisas is not applicable to injuries and explain the Hanafi ruling on a Muslim injuring a kafir, citing the evidence.

Core Idea
Qisas requires exact equivalence; when healing makes that impossible, only diyah is due.

This passage presents a key limitation on qisas: not every intentional injury triggers retaliation. When the wound heals or exact equivalence cannot be achieved, the law shifts to financial compensation (diyah). The passage also addresses the controversial issue of a Muslim injuring a kafir, relying on the hadith 'A Muslim is not killed for a disbeliever' and the precedent of Caliph Umar to deny qisas and impose diyah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the injuries and ruling
Note the two specific injuries: cutting a hand from half the forearm, and a penetrating wound (jā'ifah) that heals. The ruling for both is 'no qisas upon him'.
These are examples where exact retaliation is physically or legally impossible.
2
Understand the reason
Why is qisas not applicable? Because after healing, the wound's state changes, making precise retaliation (musawat) impossible. The principle: qisas requires exact equivalence.
The Hanafi school strictly requires musawat for qisas.
3
Examine Muslim-kafir issue
The passage extends the same principle to a Muslim injuring a kafir. The hadith 'A Muslim is not killed for a disbeliever' and Umar's ruling of diyah establish that qisas is negated.
This is a point of ikhtilaf with Shafi'i.
4
Consider opposing view and Hanafi response
Imam al-Shafi'i (as represented) holds that qisas applies for limb injury to a kafir. Hanafi responds by pointing to the explicit hadith and the precedent, and by analogy: if life is protected only by diyah, then limbs are a fortiori.
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi.
5
Apply the general principle
Ibrahim's athar states: any intentional wound where qisas cannot be exacted, compensation falls on the injurer from his own wealth, not his 'āqila.
This reinforces that diyah is wajib directly on the perpetrator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكافر ومن قطع يد رجل من نصف الساعد او جرحه جائفة فبرأ منها فلا قصاص عليه. وان لا يقتل مسلم بكافر. ترك عمر القود وقضى عليه بالدية. عن ابراهيم قال ماكان من جرح من العمد لا يستطاع فيه القصاص فهو على الجارح فى ماله دون عاقلته. عن ابراهيم كان يقال اذا كسرت اليد او الرجل ثم برأت ولم ينقص منها شيء ارشها مائة وثمانون درهما.
And the disbeliever: And whoever cuts the hand of a man from half the forearm, or wounds him with a jai'fah (penetrating wound), and he recovers from it, then there is no qisas upon him. And: A Muslim is not killed for a disbeliever. Umar left qisas and ruled diyah against him. From Ibrahim: He said, 'Whatever intentional wound in which qisas cannot be exacted, its compensation is upon the injurer from his property, not from his 'aqila.' From Ibrahim: It used to be said, 'When the hand or foot is broken, then it heals and nothing is deficient from it, its arsh (compensation) is one hundred and eighty dirhams.'
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It first states that if a person cuts off another's hand from half the forearm or inflicts a penetrating wound (jai'fah) that later heals, no qisas (retaliation) is due on the aggressor. The reason, as explained in the Urdu commentary, is that exact equivalence (musawat) is impossible since the healing process changes the nature, so only diyah (blood money) is obligatory. The passage then addresses the issue of a Muslim injuring a kafir. Based on the hadith 'A Muslim is not killed for a disbeliever' (reported by Bukhari), the Hanafi position is that qisas is not applicable; only diyah is due, as shown by the precedent of Caliph Umar who left qisas and ruled diyah. Further, the athar from Ibrahim (al-Nakha'i) confirm that for any intentional wound where qisas cannot be exacted, the compensation falls on the aggressor's own wealth, not on his 'aqila (tribal guarantors). Another athar specifies that if a limb breaks and heals without deficiency, the arsh is 180 dirhams. This lesson guards against the misunderstanding that every intentional injury entails qisas; when precise retaliation is infeasible, financial compensation replaces it.

Key Terms
جائفة (jā'ifah): A wound that penetrates into the body cavity (e.g., abdomen or chest).
قصاص (qiṣāṣ): Retaliatory punishment in kind, such as cutting off the hand for hand, or killing for killing.
ديت (diyah): Blood money, a financial compensation paid to the victim or heirs for injury or death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The aggressor must have his hand cut off in qisas.
B) The aggressor is free of any liability.
C) The aggressor must pay diyah (blood money), not qisas.
D) The aggressor must pay only a nominal fine.

Key Takeaway
Qisas is not mandatory when exact retaliation is impossible; in such cases, diyah from the aggressor's own wealth is wajib.

Qisas — Retaliation

Qisas for a Head Wound When Aggressor's Head Is Larger
Ruling on qisas when aggressor's head is larger

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule of qisas when a head wound covers the full width of the victim's head but not the aggressor's, and identify the victim's options.

Core Idea
Qisas is limited by the extent of the victim's wound, not the aggressor's larger head.

The Hanafi school prescribes exact equivalence (musawat) in qisas. This passage addresses a specific scenario: a head wound that spans the entire width of the victim's head but, due to the aggressor's larger head, does not cover the same proportion. Learn how the rule balances justice and precision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the parties
Recognise that an aggressor has inflicted a head wound (shajjah) on the victim. This is a case of intentional injury subject to qisas.
2
Determine the extent of the wound relative to the victim's head
The wound covers the entire space between the victim's two temporal bones (qarnayn). This is the condition for the special rule.
The term قَرْن (qarn) refers to the temple or side of the head.
3
Compare with the aggressor's head
The same wound does not cover the full width of the aggressor's head because his head is larger. This creates an imbalance in proportional equivalence.
4
State the ruling
The victim has a choice: (1) exact qisas to the measure of his own wound, starting from whichever side he wishes, or (2) take the full arsh (blood money) without retaliation.
The option is given due to the impossibility of exact proportionality.
5
Understand the rationale
Qisas requires exact equality (مماثلة). Inflicting a wound equal to the aggressor's larger head would exceed the victim's injury, which is unjust. Therefore, the victim is limited to his own wound's length.
This illustrates the Hanafi commitment to precise justice in qisas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن شجّ رجلا فاستوعبت الشجة مابين قرنيه وهي لا تستوعب مابين قرني الشاج فالمشجوج بالخيار ان شاء اقتص بمقدار شجته يبتدئ من اى الجانبين شاء وان شاء اخذ الارش كاملا
And whoever wounds a man's head, and the wound encompasses the entire space between his two horns (temples), while it does not encompass the space between the two horns of the aggressor, then the wounded person has the choice: if he wishes, he may exact qisas to the measure of his wound, starting from whichever side he wishes; and if he wishes, he may take the full arsh.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement. It deals with a head wound (shajjah) that covers the entire width of the victim's head between the two temporal bones (qarnayn). However, the aggressor's head is larger, so the same wound cannot cover his entire head. The victim is given an option: he may either take qisas by inflicting a wound of the same length on the aggressor's head, starting from either side, but only to the extent of his own wound; or he may forgo qisas and claim the full arsh (diyah for that wound). The reason is that qisas requires exact equivalence (musawat), and since the aggressor's head is larger, cutting across the entire width would be excessive. Therefore, the victim is restricted to the area equal to his own wound. The Urdu commentary gives an example: if the victim's forehead is 5 inches and the aggressor's is 8 inches, the victim can only wound 5 inches of the aggressor's forehead, not the full 8 inches.

Key Terms
شج (shajja): A head wound that reaches the bone.
قرن (qarn): The temporal bone or the side of the head (the 'horn' of the head).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The victim can wound the aggressor's entire head width.
B) The victim can only wound the same length as his own wound.
C) Qisas is not allowed; only diyah is due.
D) The victim can choose to wound any part of the aggressor's head.

Key Takeaway
When a head wound covers the full width of the victim's head but not the aggressor's, the victim may either take qisas limited to his own wound length or receive full arsh.

Qisas — Retaliation

No Qisas on Tongue or Penis Except for Glans
States that qisas is not applicable to tongue or penis unless glans is cut

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that qisas is not applicable to the tongue or penis unless the glans is cut, and explain the rationale.

Core Idea
Qisas requires precise equivalence; tongue and penis lack fixed shape, so only the glans qualifies.

This passage presents a clear exception in the law of qisas. While qisas (retaliation) is generally applicable to injuries to limbs, the tongue and the penis are excluded because they do not have a fixed shape for exact equivalency. However, the glans (hashafah) is a distinct part, so cutting it triggers qisas.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the rule
Read and commit to memory the Arabic text and its translation: 'ولا قصاص في اللسان ولا في الذكر الا ان يقطع الحشفة' – 'There is no qisas on the tongue nor on the penis unless the glans is cut.'
Focus on the exception phrase 'الا ان يقطع الحشفة'.
2
Understand the rationale
Understand why tongue and penis are excluded: they are not fixed in shape; they extend and contract, making exact equivalence impossible. Thus, diyah (blood money) substitutes.
This rationale is based on the principle that qisas requires precise equivalency.
3
Identify the exception
Note that for the penis, qisas becomes applicable if the glans (hashafah) is cut because it is a distinct, measurable part. The tongue has no such exception.
The commentary also mentions that cutting the testicles (khisyatayn) may also be actionable, but the text only explicitly exempts the glans.
4
Apply the rule
Given a scenario, determine whether qisas applies. For example: A man's tongue is cut – no qisas. A man's penis is cut above the glans, leaving the glans intact – no qisas. The glans itself is cut – qisas applies.
Practice with several hypotheticals to internalize the exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا قصاص في اللسان ولا في الذكر الا ان يقطع الحشفة
And there is no qisas in the tongue nor in the penis, unless the glans (al-hashafah) is cut.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement. It declares that qisas (retaliation) is not applicable for injuries to the tongue or the penis. The reason, as explained in the Urdu commentary, is that these organs are not of fixed shape; they extend and contract, making exact equivalence impossible. However, there is an exception: if the penis is cut at the glans (al-hashafah), qisas becomes possible because the glans is a distinct, measurable part. Similarly, the commentary notes that if the testicles (khisyatayn) are cut, that involves removing the entire penis from the root, which is also measurable, so qisas would apply. The exclusion of qisas means that the victim is entitled to diyah (blood money) instead. This lesson emphasizes the principle that qisas requires precise equivalency; where that is not possible, financial compensation substitutes.

Key Terms
حشفة (ḥashafah): The glans or head of the penis; a distinct part that can be measured for qisas.
ذكر (dhakar): The male genital organ (penis).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When any part of the penis is cut.
B) Only when the glans (hashafah) is cut.
C) When the entire penis is cut off.
D) Qisas never applies to the penis.

Key Takeaway
Qisas on the penis is only applicable when the glans is cut; otherwise, the remedy is diyah.

Qisas — Retaliation
intermediate
6 min
Qisas when Offender's Hand is Defective
Ruling on qisas when offender's hand is defective.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qisas when the offender's limb is defective and apply the victim's option in practice.

Core Idea
Qisas requires exact equality; if impossible, victim chooses either defective qisas or full compensation.

In Hanafi fiqh, qisas must be precisely equal. When the offender's hand is defective (e.g., paralyzed or missing fingers) and the victim's hand is sound, exact retribution is impossible. The Hanafi school grants the victim a choice: take qisas on the defective hand without further compensation, or forgo qisas and claim the full blood-money for a sound hand. This passage establishes that clear option.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Read the passage and note the two key facts: the victim's hand is sound and the offender's hand is defective (shalla or missing fingers).
The defect must be substantial, not a minor imperfection.
2
State the ruling
The victim has two choices: either cut the defective hand (qisas) and receive nothing else, or forgo qisas and take the full arsh (diyah) for a sound hand.
The choice is entirely the victim's; there is no third option.
3
Analyze the first option
If the victim chooses qisas, the defective hand is amputated, but because it is defective, the victim receives no additional compensation. Equality is approximated, not perfect.
The offender's loss is less than the victim's, but the victim voluntarily accepts this.
4
Analyze the second option
If the victim chooses arsh, he receives the full diyah for a sound hand (the value of his lost hand) and the offender retains his defective hand.
The arsh is the same as for a sound hand, because the victim's hand was sound.
5
Identify the underlying principle
The principle of musawa (equality) in qisas means you cannot take more than you lost. Since the offender's hand is defective, exact equality is unattainable, so the law gives the victim this choice.
This is a Hanafi application of the broader qisas rules.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واذا كانت يد المقطوع صحيحة ويد القاطع شلاء او ناقصة الاصابع فالمقطوع بالخيار ان شاء قطع اليد المعيبة ولا شيء له غيرها وان شاء اخذ الارش كاملا
اگر کٹا ہوا ہاتھ صحیح ہو اور کاٹنے والے کا ہاتھ شل ہو یا انگلی ناقص ہو تو مقطوع کو اختیار ہے چاہے عیب والا ہاتھ کاٹ لے اور اس کے لئے اس کے علاوہ کچھ نہیں ملے گا اور چاہے تو پوری دیت لے لے۔
جس کا ہاتھ کاٹا اسکا ہاتھ صحیح تھا اور جس نے کاٹا اس کا ہاتھ شل تھا یا اس کی انگلی خراب تھی تو جس کا ہاتھ کا ٹا اس کو دو اختیار ہیں ۔ ہاتھ کاٹنے والے کا معیوب ہاتھ قصاص میں کاٹ لے۔ اس صورت میں اس نے قصاص لے لیا اس لئے اس کو دیت نہیں ملے گی ۔ یا عیب دار ہاتھ کی کچھ تم نہیں ملے گی۔ اور دوسری صورت یہ ہے کہ اپنے ہاتھ کی پوری دبیت وصول کرے۔ شٹل ہاتھ کو نہ کاٹے۔
اس کے پاس عیب دار ہا تھ ہی ہے تو کیا کاٹے گا ؟ کاٹنا ہے تو وہی کاٹے یا پھر پوری دیت لے لے۔
If the victim's hand is sound and the offender's hand is paralyzed or has missing fingers, then the victim has the choice: if he wishes, he may cut the defective hand and there is nothing else for him; if he wishes, he may take the full blood-money. Urdu commentary explains the same.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage states a ruling on qisas when there is a defect in the offender's limb. The basic principle is that qisas requires equality (musawa). If the offender's hand is defective (e.g., paralyzed or missing fingers), cutting it in qisas would not achieve exact equality because the victim's hand is sound. The Hanafi school grants the victim an option: either he may take qisas on the defective hand (and forgo any additional compensation), or he may forgo qisas and claim the full arsh (blood-money) for his sound hand. The Urdu commentary explains that if the victim chooses qisas, he gets no diyah; if he chooses diyah, he gets the full amount for a sound hand. This is based on the principle that qisas must be equal, and when equality is impossible, the victim may claim monetary compensation.

Key Terms
يد المقطوع (yad al-maqtu'): The hand of the person whose hand was cut off (the victim).
يد القاطع (yad al-qati'): The hand of the person who cut off the victim's hand (the offender).
الارش (al-arsh): Blood-money or compensation for a bodily injury; in this context, the full diyah for a sound hand.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He takes the offender's paralyzed hand and also gets full diyah.
B) He takes the offender's paralyzed hand and gets no diyah.
C) He cannot take qisas because the limbs are not equal.
D) He takes the offender's sound hand if available.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi qisas, when the offender's limb is defective and the victim's limb is sound, the victim may choose either defective qisas or full arsh, but not both.

Qisas — Retaliation
intermediate

Qisas for Head Wound: Equality When Heads Differ in Size
Ruling on qisas for a head wound that spans only part of the offender's head.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qisas for a head wound when the victim's head is smaller than the offender's, and identify the options available to the victim.

Core Idea
Qisas requires equality in extent, not full head coverage. Victim chooses limited qisas or full arsh.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in qisas for head wounds (shajjah). When the victim's head is smaller than the offender's, exact replication of the wound across the full width of the head is impossible. The Hanafi school provides a practical solution that balances the right to retaliation with the requirement of equality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is giving an intentional head wound (shajjah). The ruling is that qisas is obligatory upon demand, but with strict equality conditions.
The initial ruling is the same as general qisas: wajib for 'amad.
2
Recognize the problem of unequal head size
If the victim's head is smaller so that his wound covers his full temporal width, but the offender's head is larger, exact replication across the full width is impossible on the offender.
The key phrase is "it does not span the entire width between the offender's temporal bones."
3
State the victim's options
The victim may either: (a) take qisas to the extent of his own wound (not the offender's full head) from any side he chooses, or (b) forgo qisas and take full arsh (blood money).
The choice belongs entirely to the victim.
4
Apply the rule with an example
Use the Urud example: Zayd's forehead is 5 inches, Umar's is 8 inches. If Zayd wants qisas, he can only cut 5 inches on Umar, not the full 8, to maintain equality.
The qisas wound must equal the victim's wound length, not the offender's head size.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن شجّ رجلا فاستوعبت الشجة مابين قرنيه وهي لا تستوعب مابين قرني الشاج فالمشجوج بالخيار ان شاء اقتص بمقدار شجته يبتدئ من اى الجانبين شاء وان شاء اخذ الارش کاملا
مثلا زید کی پیشانی پانچ انچ لمبی ہے اس کو عمر نے زخمی کر دیا اور پورے پانچ انچ گھیر لیا اور عمر کی پیشانی آٹھ انچ لمبی ہے۔ اب زید قصاص لینا چاہتا ہے تو عمر کی پیشانی میں سے پانچ انچ زخمی کرے۔ آٹھ انچ زخمی نہ کرے تا کہ برابر سرابر ہو جائے۔ اور چاہے دائیں جانب سے زخمی کرے چاہے بائیں جانب سے زخمی کرے۔ اور اگر زخمی نہیں کرنا چاہتا ہے تو پوری دیت لے لے۔
Whoever gives a head wound to a man such that the wound spans the entire width between his temporal bones, but it does not span the entire width between the offender's temporal bones, then the wounded person has the choice: if he wishes, he may take qisas to the extent of his wound, starting from whichever side he wishes; if he wishes, he may take full arsh. The Urdu commentary gives an example of forehead lengths.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage deals with qisas for head wounds (shajjah). The key condition is that qisas must be equal. If the victim's head is smaller so that his wound covers the full width between his temples, but the offender's head is larger so that the same wound would not cover the full width, then exact qisas is not possible because the wound cannot be replicated to the same extent. The Hanafi ruling gives the victim an option: he may take qisas on the offender's head to the extent of his own wound (e.g., if his wound is 5 inches, he can inflict a 5-inch wound on the offender's forehead, even though it does not span the full width), starting from any side, and then he gets no additional compensation; or he may forgo qisas and take the full arsh for the injury. The Urdu commentary illustrates with an example: Zayd's forehead is 5 inches, Umar's is 8 inches; if Zayd wants qisas, he can only cut 5 inches on Umar, not the full 8, to maintain equality.

Key Terms
الشجة (al-shajjah): A head wound that reaches the bone or deeper; in this context, a wound on the head.
القرنيين (al-qarnayn): The two temporal bones or sides of the head; the width between them.
الاقتصاص (al-iqtisas): Retaliatory retaliation, i.e., inflicting the same injury as the offender caused.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a victim has a 2-inch head wound that covers the full width of his 2-inch forehead, and the offender has a 4-inch forehead, what is the maximum qisas the victim can take?
A) 2 inches
B) 4 inches
C) 1 inch
D) No qisas is possible

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi qisas, equality is measured by the actual extent of the injury, not by anatomical coverage; the victim may choose limited qisas or full arsh.

The Hanafi Exception for the Glans in Qisas
Ruling that no qisas is possible in tongue and penis unless the entire glans is cut.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qisas for the tongue and penis, explain the rationale of elasticity, and identify the exception for the glans.

Core Idea
Qisas requires equal retaliation — elastic organs make it impossible, so only diyah applies unless the stable glans is cut.

This lesson examines a specific Hanafi ruling from the chapter on jinayat (injuries). The text states that qisas (retaliation) is not applicable for injuries to the tongue and penis because these organs expand and contract, making it impossible to inflict an exactly equal wound. However, if the glans (hashafah) is cut off, qisas may be applied because that part does not contract. We will explore the legal reasoning and the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
What is being ruled upon? The act of cutting the tongue or penis. The ruling: no qisas (retaliation) applies; instead, diyah (blood money) is due.
This is the default hukm.
2
Understand the Rationale
Why is qisas impossible? Because these organs stretch and shrink (madd wa jazb). Exact equal retaliation is physically unattainable.
The Urdu commentary explicitly mentions 'they become long and shrink'.
3
Identify the Exception
When does qisas become possible? When the entire glans (hashafah) is cut off. The glans is stable and does not contract, so equal retaliation is feasible.
The Arabic text says 'illa an yaqta'a al-hashafah'.
4
Apply the Principle to a Related Case
The commentary adds: If the testicles are also cut, the penis is severed from the root, which is also a stable point, so qisas applies there as well.
This expands the exception, but the core principle is stability.
5
Note the Authority
This ruling is based on the Hanafi school's principle of qisas requiring exact equivalence (mumathala). The passage references an athar from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i.
The athar is not fully quoted but supports the Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا قصاص في اللسان ولا في الذكر الا ان يقطع الحشفة .
زبان میں اور ذکر میں قصاص نہیں ہے مگر یہ کہ حشفہ کاٹ دے۔ ذکر اور زبان لمبے ہوتے ہیں اور سکڑتے ہیں۔ اس لئے ان کو برابر سرابر کا ناممکن نہیں ہے۔ اس لئے اگر اس کو کاٹ دے تو ان میں قصاص نہیں ہے دیت ہے۔ ہاں خصیتین بھی کاٹ دے تو اس صورت میں ذکر بالکل جڑ سے کٹ جاتا ہے جہاں سے سکڑتا نہیں ہے۔ اس لئے وہاں سے قصاص لینا ممکن ہے۔ اس لئے خصیتین کاٹ دے تو قصاص لیا جائے گا (۲) اوپر اثر گزر چکا ہے۔ عن ابراهيم قال ما كان من
There is no qisas in the tongue nor in the penis, unless he cuts the glans. Urdu commentary explains: tongue and penis are elongated and contract, so equalization is impossible, hence no qisas, only diyah. But if the testicles are also cut, then the penis is cut off completely from the root where it does not contract, so qisas is possible there. An athar from Ibrahim is above.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage states the Hanafi ruling that qisas (retaliation) is not applicable for injuries to the tongue and penis because these organs can stretch and contract, making it impossible to inflict an exactly equal wound. Instead, diyah (monetary compensation) is given. However, if the entire glans (hashafah) is cut off, qisas may be applied because that part does not contract. The Urdu commentary adds that if the testicles are also cut, then the penis is severed from the root, which is stable, so qisas becomes possible. The passage ends with a reference to a previous athar from Ibrahim (likely Ibrahim al-Nakha'i) stating a similar principle.

Key Terms
اللسان (al-lisan): The tongue; a body part where qisas is not possible due to its elasticity.
الذكر (al-dhakar): The male organ (penis); also ineligible for qisas due to change in size.
الحشفة (al-hashafah): The glans of the penis; cutting it allows qisas because it does not contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because the tongue can stretch and contract, making equal retaliation impossible.
C) Because the tongue is considered internal and not subject to qisas.
D) Because qisas only applies to limbs mentioned in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, qisas for tongue and penis is impossible due to elasticity; only the glans is stable enough for equal retaliation.

Qisas — Retaliation

Hukm of 'Amad Where Qisas is Impossible
A marginal note on liability for intentional injuries where qisas is impossible

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for intentional injuries where qisas is impossible and distinguish the liable party from accidental cases.

Core Idea
When qisas is impossible for intentional harm, the offender alone pays diyah from his property.

This marginal note from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i clarifies a key Hanafi rule regarding liability for intentional injuries. While the 'aqilah (family) bears diyah for unintentional killings, intentional acts shift the financial burden to the offender personally when retaliation is not feasible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The passage refers to an intentional wound ('amad) for which qisas (retaliation) cannot be carried out due to physical impossibility (e.g., because the organ cannot be equalized).
2
Understand the default rule
For intentional injury where qisas is possible, the primary punishment is retaliation (qisas) on the injurer. This is the starting point.
Qisas is a right of the victim.
3
Recognize the exception
When qisas is impossible, the obligation shifts to diyah (monetary compensation). The passage states this clearly.
Impossibility is a condition for switching from qisas to diyah.
4
Identify the liable party
The indemnity (ta'wan) is upon the injurer himself, from his own wealth, NOT from his 'aqilah (family/ tribe).
This contrasts with accidental injuries where 'aqilah is liable.
5
Reason for the distinction
The 'aqilah is only responsible for unintentional homicides/injuries. Intentional acts are personal, so the offender bears full responsibility.
This is a consistent Hanafi principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حاشیه : (الف) حضرت ابراهیم نے فرمایا جان بوجھ کر جو زخم کیا ہو جس میں قصاص لینا ممکن نہ ہو تو اس کا تاوان زخم کرنے والے پر ہے اس کے مال میں نہ کہ اس کے خاندان پر
Marginal note (A): Hadrat Ibrahim said: For an intentional wound in which qisas is not possible, its indemnity is upon the injurer, from his wealth, not from his family ('aqilah).
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This marginal note (hāshiyah) records a statement from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i (a prominent tabi'i) regarding intentional injuries (al-'amad) where qisas cannot be implemented due to physical impossibility (e.g., because the organ cannot be equalized). In such cases, the compensation (ta'wan = indemnity) is not on the killer's tribe or family ('aqilah) as is the case for unintentional killings, but rather it is on the injurer himself, from his own property. This underscores the Hanafi rule that when qisas is unavailable, the offender bears full financial responsibility.

Key Terms
العمد الذي لا يستطاع فيه القصاص (al-'amdu alladhi la yustata'u fihi al-qisas): An intentional injury for which retaliation (qisas) is physically impossible, e.g., due to the nature of the organ.
العاقلة (al-'aqilah): The offender's tribe or paternal relatives who traditionally bear the diyah in cases of accidental killing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the hashiya, who pays the indemnity for an intentional wound that cannot be subject to qisas?
A) The offender's family ('aqilah).
B) The offender himself from his property.
C) The state treasury (Bayt al-Mal).
D) No indemnity is paid.

Key Takeaway
For intentional injuries where qisas is impossible, the offender is personally liable for diyah from his own property, not his 'aqilah.

Qisas — Retaliation

Settlement in Qisas: Dropping Retaliation with Property
Ruling with proof from Quran and Sunnah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on settlement (sulh) between the killer and heirs in intentional murder and apply the proofs from Quran and Sunnah.

Core Idea
Settlement with any property, little or much, drops qisas and obligates the agreed amount.

In intentional murder, qisas (retaliation) is the default right of the heirs. However, Islamic law encourages reconciliation. This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling that if the killer and heirs mutually agree on a settlement involving property, qisas is dropped and the agreed property becomes wajib upon the killer. The amount can be less or more than the full blood-money (diya). We will also explore the evidence from the Quran and a hadith that support this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Read the passage carefully. The ruling states that when the killer (qatil) and the heirs (awliya) settle on property (mal), qisas falls (saqata) and the property becomes wajib. Note that the amount is irrelevant: 'qalilan kana aw kathiran' (whether little or much).
This applies specifically to intentional murder (qatl amd).
2
Understand the Legal Logic
In intentional murder, qisas is the original obligation. The settlement substitutes a property obligation. Unlike accidental killing where heirs cannot take more than diya, here they can take any amount because they are compensating for the right to qisas.
3
Locate the First Proof: Quran 2:178
The verse prescribes qisas but adds: 'faman ufiya lahu min akhihi shay'un' (if anything is remitted by the brother). This indicates permission to pardon. The subsequent command 'fittiba'un bil ma'ruf' (follow up with fairness) implies a financial settlement.
The phrase 'shay'un' (anything) supports the Hanafi position that the settlement amount can be any property.
4
Examine the Second Proof: Hadith
The hadith of Abu Hurairah states that the heir has two choices: either accept blood-money (diya) or have the killer executed (iqad). This confirms the option to settle for property, and the commentary notes that pardoning is better as it spares the killer's life.
This hadith is from Bukhari and Abu Dawud, indicating its authenticity.
5
Apply the Ruling
Consider a scenario: A man intentionally kills another. His heirs negotiate with the killer and agree on a payment of 50,000 dirhams (less than full diya). According to this ruling, qisas is dropped and the killer must pay the agreed amount. The heirs cannot later demand qisas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واذا اصطلح القاتل واولياء المقتول على مال سقط القصاص ووجب المال قليلا كان او كثيرا (۲۳۲۶) (۳۳) اگر قاتل اور مقتول کے ورثہ صلح کر لیں کسی مال پر تو قصاص ساقط ہو جائے گا اور مال واجب ہوگا۔ مال کم ہو یا زیادہ۔ الشرق قاتل نے قتل عمد کیا تھا جس کی وجہ سے قصاص لازم تھا لیکن قاتل اور مقتول کے ورثہ نے کسی مال پر صلح کر لی تو اب قصاص ساقط ہو جائے گا۔ اور قاتل پر وہ مال لازم ہوگا جو صلح میں طے ہوا۔ وہ مال دیت کاملہ سے کم ہو یا زیادہ۔ قتل خطاء یا قتل شبه عمد ہو تو مقتول کے ورثہ دیت سے زیادہ نہیں لے سکتے ۔ لیکن یہاں تو قصاص لازم تھا اس لئے اب صلح میں جو طے ہو وہ دینا ہوگا (۲) آیت میں ہے۔ یا ایها الذین آمنوا كتب عليكم القصاص في القتلى الحر بالحر والعبد بالعبد والانثى بالانثى فمن عفى له من اخيه شيء فاتباع بالمعروف واداء اليه باحسان (ب) (آیت ۷۸ اسورۃ البقرۃ ۲) اس آیت سے معلوم ہوا کہ قصاص معاف کر کے مال پر صلح کر سکتا ہے (۲) حدیث میں ہے۔ حدثنا ابو هريرة ... ومن قتل له قتيل فهو بخير النظرين اما يودى واما يقاد (ج) ( بخاری شریف، باب من قتل له قتیل فهو بخير انظرین ص ۱۰۱۶ نمبر ۶۸۸۰ رابوداؤد شریف، باب الامام مر بالعفونی الدم ص ۲۷۰ نمبر ۴۴۹۶) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ قصاص معاف کر کے مال پر صلح کر سکتا ہے بلکہ یہ بہتر ہے کیونکہ قاتل کی جان بچے گی۔
And if the killer and the heirs of the killed settle on property, qisas falls and the property becomes wajib, whether little or much. (2326) (33) If the killer and the heirs of the killed make a settlement on some property, then qisas will be dropped and the property will be wajib. The property may be little or much. Explanation: The killer had committed intentional murder which necessitated qisas, but when the killer and the heirs made a settlement on some property, then qisas will be dropped and the property agreed upon in the settlement will be obligatory on the killer. That property may be less than full blood-money or more. In accidental killing or quasi-intentional killing, the heirs cannot take more than the blood-money. But here, since qisas was obligatory, whatever is agreed in the settlement must be given. (2) In the verse: 'O you who believe! Retaliation is prescribed for you regarding the killed: free for free, slave for slave, female for female. But if anything is remitted by the brother [of the victim], then follow up with fairness and payment with goodly gratitude.' (Surah Al-Baqarah 2:178) From this verse, it is understood that qisas can be pardoned and settlement on property can be made. (2) In the hadith: Abu Hurairah narrated... 'Whoever has a relative killed, he has two options: either to accept blood-money or to have the killer executed.' (Bukhari, chapter: Whoever has a relative killed, he has the two options, p.1016, no.6880; Abu Dawud, chapter: The imam's order to pardon blood, p.270, no.4496) From this hadith, it is understood that qisas can be pardoned and settlement on property can be made; rather it is better because the killer's life will be spared.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Quran and Sunnah. The ruling states that if the killer and the heirs of the victim mutually agree on a settlement involving property (mal), regardless of the amount (little or much), the obligation of qisas is dropped and the agreed property becomes wajib upon the killer. The commentary explains that this applies specifically to intentional murder where qisas was originally obligatory. The settlement amount can be less than or more than the full diya (blood-money). In contrast, for accidental or quasi-intentional killing, the heirs cannot take more than the prescribed diya. The proof is from Quran 2:178, which permits remission and subsequent fair settlement, and from a hadith granting the heirs the choice between qisas or blood-money. The commentary also notes that pardoning and settling is better as it spares the killer's life.

Key Terms
قصاص (qisas): Retaliation; the legal right to take a life for a life in cases of intentional murder.
صلح (sulh): An amicable settlement between the killer and heirs, typically involving payment of property in lieu of qisas.
دية (diya): Blood-money; the fixed financial compensation for homicide or injury payable to the heirs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) The heirs can still demand qisas after the settlement.
D) The killer must pay the full diya regardless.

Key Takeaway
In intentional murder, the killer and heirs can settle for any property to drop qisas, and the agreed amount becomes wajib.

Qisas — Retaliation
intermediate
8 min
Pardon by One Heir Drops Qisas for All
Ruling with proof on pardon by a single heir

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the effect of one heir's pardon or settlement on the right to qisas and explain the evidence from hadith.

Core Idea
A single heir's pardon extinguishes the right to qisas for all heirs; only diya remains.

In Hanafi law, the right to qisas (retaliation) for murder or wound is a collective right of the victim's heirs. However, this lesson reveals a key rule: if even one heir pardons (afw) or settles his share for compensation, the entire claim to qisas is dropped. Only diya (blood-money) remains, payable from the killer's own wealth. This binding effect is based on a hadith and demonstrates the Hanafi preference for preserving life and financial settlement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being described (pardon or settlement by one heir) and what legal effect it produces (qisas dropped, diya for remaining shares).
The text explicitly says: 'So if one of the partners pardoned the blood or settled his share for compensation, then the remaining right from qisas is dropped.'
2
Locate the dalil
Find the proof cited: a hadith from Aisha (ra) where the Prophet (saw) said that those who are killed should be restrained step by step, even if a woman (pardons).
The hadith text: 'على المقتتلين أن ينحجزوا الأول فالاول وإن كانت امرأة'
3
Understand the wajh al-dalalah
Analyze how the hadith supports the ruling. The phrase 'ينحجزوا' implies restraint even if the pardoning heir is a woman, showing that her pardon binds others.
The Hanafi interpretation is that the right of qisas is entire; once part is relinquished, the whole drops.
4
Note the cross-madhhab ikhtilaf
Some other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) hold that remaining heirs can still demand qisas for their shares. Identify the difference.
Hanafis consider the right indivisible; others see each heir holding a separate right.
5
State the practical outcome
The killer pays diya only for the unpardoned shares, out of his own wealth, not from his clan (a'qila).
This is because the injury/wound is deliberate but qisas is impossible due to pardon.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فان عفا احد الشركاء من الدم او صالح من جرح من العمد لا يستطاع فيه القصاص فهو على الجارح في ماله دون عاقلته (الف) (مصنف ابن ابی شیبۃ ۱۰۱ العمد الذي لا يستطاع فيه القصاص ج خامس، ص۲۰۳، نمبر ۲۷۴۰۴) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ جس عضو کا برابر سرا بر قصاص لینا ناممکن ہو اس میں قصاص نہیں ہے دیت ہے۔ [۲۳۲۷] (۳۴) پس اگر شریک میں سے کسی ایک نے خون معاف کر دیا یا اپنے حصے پر صلح کر لی عوض کے بدلے تو قصاص سے باقی حق ساقط ہو جائے گا اور ان کے لئے باقی حصے ہوں گے دیت سے۔ تنشر مقتول کے ورثہ میں سے کسی ایک نے اپنا حصہ معاف کر دیا یا اپنے حصے کے بدلے قاتل سے صلح کر لی تو باقی ورثہ کو قصاص لینے کا حق نہیں رہے گا۔ بلکہ دیت میں سے جو حصے ان کے حق میں آئیں گے وہ لے۔ صلى الله حدثني عائشة زوج النبي ان رسول الله قال على المقتتلين ان ينحجزوا الاول فالاول وان كانت امرأة حاشیه : (الف) حضرت ابراہیم نے فرمایا جان بوجھ کر جوز ختم کیا ہو جس میں قصاص لینا ممکن نہ ہو تو اس کا تاوان زخم کرنے والے پر ہے اس کے مال میں نہ کہ اس کے خاندان پر (ب) اے ایمان والو تم پر قصاص فرض ہے مقتول کے بارے میں آزاد آزاد کے بدلے ، غلام غلام کے بدلے، مونٹ مونث کے بدلے۔ پس جس نے اپنے بھائی کی جانب سے معاف کر دیا تو معروف کے ساتھ وصول کرتا ہے اور اس کی طرف احسان کے ساتھ ادا کرنا ہے (ج) کسی نے کسی کو قتل کیا تو اس کو دو اختیار ہیں۔ یا دیت ادا کرے یا قصاص لے۔
If one of the partners pardons the blood or settles from a deliberate wound that cannot be subjected to qisas, then it is upon the injurer in his wealth, not upon his tribe. (A) (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba...) From this athar it is understood that for a body part for which exact qisas is impossible, there is no qisas; there is diya. [2327] (34) So if one of the partners pardoned the blood or settled his share for compensation, then the remaining right from qisas is dropped, and they will have their remaining shares from the diya. Explanation: If one of the heirs of the killed pardoned his share or settled with the killer for his share, then the remaining heirs will not have the right to take qisas. Rather, they will take the shares that come to them from the diya. Aisha narrated that the Messenger of Allah said: 'Those who are killed should be restrained, the first then the next, even if a woman.' Marginal notes: (A) Ibrahim said: For an intentional wound where qisas is not possible, the compensation is on the injurer in his wealth, not on his tribe. (B) O you who believe! Retaliation is prescribed for you... (C) Whoever has a relative killed, he has two options.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage contains a ruling and its proof. The ruling states that if one of the heirs (shuraka') pardons the blood (i.e., the right to qisas) or settles his share for compensation, then the right to qisas for all is dropped, and the remaining heirs only take their respective shares of the diya. The text also includes a separate related ruling about intentional wounds where qisas is impossible; such wounds are compensable by diya from the injurer's own wealth, not his clan. The proofs include an athar from Ibrahim, the Quranic verse (2:178) showing that pardon leads to financial settlement, and a hadith giving heirs the choice between qisas and diya. The commentary explains that once one heir pardons or settles, qisas is no longer available; only diya remains.

Key Terms
شركاء (shuraka): The heirs of the victim who share the right to qisas or diya.
عفو (afw): Pardon; voluntarily relinquishing one's right to qisas.
صلح (sulh): Settlement; the heir reaches a financial agreement with the killer for his share.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Multiple Killers for One Victim: All Executed
Ruling with proof from Sahaba

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on multiple intentional killers of one victim and explain the proof from the athar of Umar.

Core Idea
All participants in an intentional murder are equally liable to qisas, even if the victim is only one.

The Hanafi school holds a distinctive position in qisas: when a group intentionally kills a single person, every member of the group is executed. This rule is derived from the practice of Caliph Umar, who declared that if all the people of San'a' had participated in a murder, he would kill them all. In this lesson, we examine the ruling, its proof, and the underlying principle of joint liability.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
Identify the exact hukm: when a group kills one person intentionally, qisas is taken from all of them. Note that this is the mufta bihi position.
The ruling is unconditional — it applies regardless of how many participants there are.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites an athar from Ibn Umar reporting that Umar said he would kill all participants, even if the entire population of San'a' were involved.
This is a qawl sahabi (statement of a Companion) and is considered a valid proof in Hanafi usul.
3
Analyse the wajh al-dalalah
The operative phrase is 'law ishtaraku fiha ahl San'a laqataltuhum' — if all had participated, all would be killed. This indicates that participation, not the number of victims, triggers qisas.
The proof is explicit in establishing joint liability.
4
Note the cross-madhhab disagreement
The Shafi'i school generally holds that only one person can be executed for one murder, based on the principle of proportionality. The Hanafi school prioritizes the evidence of joint participation.
This ikhtilaf has practical consequences in multi-assailant cases.
5
Derive the principle
The underlying principle: each participant is a direct cause of death, and the singularity of the victim does not limit liability.
This aligns with the Hanafi emphasis on causality over strict proportionality.

Source Text

This ruling states that if multiple persons jointly commit intentional murder of a single victim, all of them are liable to qisas and will be executed. The proof is an athar from Ibn Umar reporting that Umar said if all the people of San'a' participated in killing one person, he would kill them all. The underlying principle is that each participant is directly responsible for the murder, and the singularity of the victim does not limit the number of executions.

One Killer for Multiple Victims: Single Execution
Ruling on one killer for multiple victims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when one person kills multiple victims and explain the basis for denying additional diya.

Core Idea
One execution for multiple victims – the killer’s life satisfies all qisas rights.

This lesson addresses a specific scenario in qisas (retributive justice): what happens when a single person intentionally kills multiple victims? The Hanafi school holds a clear principle – the killer is executed only once, and no additional blood money (diya) is due to the other victims' heirs. We will explore the ruling, its scriptural basis, and the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario
Identify the act: a single person ('ahad) intentionally kills a group (jamaa'ah) of victims.
2
State the ruling
The killer is executed once for all victims collectively; the heirs of all victims jointly have the right to have him executed.
No additional diya is due.
3
Identify the proof
The Quranic verse 'No bearer of burdens shall bear the burden of another' (Surah Al-An'am 6:164) is cited – the killer's single life cannot be multiplied to satisfy multiple claims.
4
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Some schools argue that after execution, diya should be paid for the remaining victims, but the Hanafis reject this, holding that the execution is the complete discharge.
The dispute is legitimate; the Hanafi position is mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if one person kills a group intentionally, there is nothing for the families of the killed besides that. [2329] (36) If one person kills a group and the heirs of the killed come, then this one execution is for the entire group, and they have nothing besides that. One person killed an entire group, so that same person will be executed for the entire group. And they will not receive anything else. Since the same person killed the entire group, he is the one responsible for qisas. Therefore, only he will be executed. And since there is no diya in this, the remaining people will not get diya either. And if they settle on diya, the property agreed upon will be divided equally among all. 'No bearer of burdens shall bear the burden of another.' (Surah Al-An'am 6:164)
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This ruling states that if a single person intentionally kills multiple victims, he is executed only once for all of them. The heirs of all victims collectively have the right to have him executed, but no additional compensation (diya) is due beyond that. If they choose to settle for property instead, the amount is divided equally among all heirs. The proof cited is the Quranic verse that no soul bears the burden of another, implying that the killer's single life suffices for all victims.

Key Terms
جماعة (jamaa'ah): A group of victims killed by one person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, one execution suffices for multiple victims – the killer's single life satisfies all qisas claims; no additional diya is due.

Collective Right of Qisas: Effect of Execution by One Heir
Ruling on collective right of qisas with proof from Quran

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that when one heir executes the murderer, the other heirs lose all rights to qisas and diyah, and explain the Quranic proof (Surah al-Isra 17:33).

Core Idea
When one heir executes the murderer, the right of all other heirs to qisas or diyah is dropped entirely.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in the law of qisas: what happens when there are multiple victims and only one heir executes the murderer? The Hanafi school holds a firm view that once the murderer is put to death by any single heir, the claims of all other heirs—whether for retaliation or blood money—are extinguished. This ruling is grounded in the Quranic prohibition against exceeding the limits in retaliation, which confines punishment to the murderer alone.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage addresses the case of multiple victims (e.g., eight men killed by one murderer). The ruling is that if one heir of a victim executes the murderer, the right of all other heirs to qisas or diyah is dropped.
This is a consequential ruling about the satisfaction of a collective right.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites Quran 17:33: 'And do not kill the soul which Allah has forbidden except by right... so let him not exceed in killing.' This establishes that retaliation must be limited to the murderer alone.
The verse is used to prohibit killing anyone other than the murderer.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'fala yusrif' (let him not exceed) indicates that once the murderer is killed, no further killing is allowed. Thus, the other heirs cannot retaliate against anyone, not even the executor heir.
The argument is that the right of qisas is a single collective right; once fulfilled, it is exhausted.
4
Note the cross-madhhab ikhtilaf
Some other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may hold that the remaining heirs can still claim diyah from the executor. The Hanafi position is that the right is entirely dropped.
The practical difference: in the Hanafi school, the executor keeps the murderer's estate (if any) and owes nothing to the others.

Source Text

A group, and the guardians of the murdered ones came, the murderer is killed for all of them, and there is nothing for them besides that. [2330] (37) If one of them comes, he kills him for himself, and the right of the remaining is dropped. This verse indicates that the penalty of one person does not fall on others. [2330] (37) If one of the heirs of the murdered comes and kills him for himself, the right of the remaining is dropped. For example, Zayd had killed eight men, so Zayd would be killed on behalf of the heirs of the eight. But if the heir of one murdered kills Zayd for himself, the heirs of the remaining murdered get nothing, and they cannot kill anyone. Because the murderer has left the world, from whom will they take qisas or wealth? Therefore, the right of the heirs of the remaining seven is dropped. (2) In the verse, it is that in the time of Jahiliyyah, they used to kill tens for one killer, so Allah forbade them from that killing and said: only the murderer should be killed. The verse is: 'And do not kill the soul which Allah has forbidden except by right. And whoever is killed unjustly, We have given his heir authority, so let him not exceed in killing; indeed, he is aided.' (Surah al-Isra, 17:33) In this verse, it is that one should not exceed in killing, i.e., not kill anyone other than the murderer. Therefore, the right of the heirs of the remaining murdered is dropped.

Commentary
This passage presents a Hanafi ruling on qisas when multiple victims exist. The matn states that if the guardians (heirs) of several murdered persons are present, the murderer is executed for all collectively, and they receive nothing else. However, if a single heir comes and executes the killer, his action fulfills the qisas for himself, and the rights of the other heirs—whether to qisas or diyah—are dropped. The Urdu commentary clarifies this with an example: if Zayd kills eight men, normally he would be executed on behalf of all eight heirs. But if one heir kills him first, the other seven can take nothing because the murderer is gone and the Quranic prohibition against israf (excess) in killing (17:33) forbids killing anyone else. The ruling is based on the principle that qisas is a collective right of all heirs; once fulfilled by one, it is satisfied for all. This is derived from the Quranic command not to exceed in retaliation, which limits punishment to the murderer alone.

Key Terms
اولياء المقتولين (awliya' al-maqtulin): The guardians or heirs of the murdered persons who have the right to demand qisas or diyah.
قصاص (qisas): Retributive justice; the execution of the murderer as a legal penalty.
دية (diyah): Blood money; financial compensation paid in lieu of qisas.

D) They can demand the state execute another person.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the right to qisas is collective among heirs; once any one heir executes the murderer, all other heirs lose any claim to qisas or diyah.

Qisas Nullified Upon Death of the Liable Person
Ruling on qisas nullified upon death of the murderer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qisas when the murderer dies and explain why no diyah is due from the estate.

Core Idea
Qisas drops with the death of the murderer; no substitute penalty applies.

This lesson addresses a critical point in Islamic criminal law: what happens when the person who must be executed for murder dies before the sentence is carried out? The Hanafi ruling is clear and firm, based on the principle that qisas is a personal right that cannot be transferred to heirs or others.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The passage states: if qisas becomes obligatory on someone and that person dies, the qisas obligation is dropped. Confirm the ruling from the text.
The ruling applies to intentional murder (qatl al-'amd).
2
Consider the basis
The passage refers to the Quranic prohibition (17:33) that no soul should be killed except by right. Since the killer is dead, no other person can be executed.
Also the principle 'No soul bears another's burden' (35:18).
3
Distinguish from unintentional killing
If the killing was unintentional (khata'), diyah is due from the murderer's estate. But for intentional murder, diyah is not due from the estate upon the killer's death.
The ruling is specific to qisas for intentional murder.
4
Apply the ruling
In practice, if the convicted murderer dies naturally before execution, the victim's family cannot claim qisas or diyah. The right to retribution terminates.
This is the mufta bihi (operative) position in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

According to the above verse, one cannot take qisas or diyah from others because the murder is not unintentional; therefore, qisas is dropped.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage states a clear Hanafi ruling: if the person who is liable for qisas (i.e., the murderer) dies, the qisas obligation is nullified. The Urdu commentary explains that since the killer is dead, there is no one to exact qisas from, and according to the Quranic prohibition (17:33), no other person can be punished. The death must be natural, not a pre-emptive execution. This ruling applies only to intentional murder (qatl al-'amd); in cases of unintentional killing, diyah is due from the estate. The principle is that qisas is a personal penalty that cannot be transferred to heirs or others.

Key Terms
قصاص (qisas): Retributive justice; execution of the murderer as a legal penalty.
سقط (saqata): Falls, drops, becomes null; a legal term indicating the termination of a right or obligation.

Key Takeaway

Joint Offenders in Qisas: Two Men Cutting One Hand
Ruling on two men cutting the same hand: no qisas, shared diyah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on joint offenders in qisas and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the narration of 'Ali.

Core Idea
When two men cut one hand, qisas is replaced by shared diyah to avoid excess and arbitrary selection.

This passage presents a classic inter-madhhab dispute in retributive justice. The Hanafi school holds that when multiple offenders cause a single injury, qisas (amputation) is not applied; instead, diyah (blood money) is divided equally. The Shafi'i school argues for qisas on both. Understanding the reasoning behind each position sharpens your ability to apply the principle of proportionality in Islamic criminal law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi ruling
What is the legal consequence for two men who cut one man's hand according to the Hanafi school? Note that neither undergoes qisas, and they jointly pay diyah of one hand, each half.
The ruling is based on avoiding disproportionality and arbitrary selection (tarjih bila murajjih).
2
Examine the Hanafi proof
The text cites a narration from al-Sha'bi about 'Ali ibn Abi Talib. How does 'Ali's judgment support the Hanafi position? He imposed diyah on false witnesses, not qisas.
The narration is a mursal hadith, but accepted in fiqh reasoning.
3
Analyze the Shafi'i opposition
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that both offenders' hands should be cut. What is his proof? He relies on 'Ali's statement: 'If I knew you did it deliberately, I would have cut both your hands.'
This is the same narration but interpreted differently.
4
Understand the Hanafi rebuttal
How does the Hanafi school respond? They argue that 'Ali's actual judgment (diyah) takes precedence over his hypothetical statement. Also, cutting both hands for one hand is disproportional.
The principle of Qisas requires exact equivalence; two hands for one hand is excessive.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference? Under Hanafi law, joint offenders pay diyah only; under Shafi'i, each undergoes qisas, resulting in two hands amputated for one.
This affects both the severity of punishment and victim's compensation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۳۳۲] (۳۹) واذا قطع رجلان يد رجل واحد فلا قصاص على كل واحد منهما وعليهما نصف الدية. 
اس آیت سے معلوم ہوا کہ ایک آدمی کا جرمانہ دوسروں پر نہیں ہوگا۔
[۲۳۳۲] (۳۹) اگر دو آدمیوں نے ایک آدمی کا ہاتھ کاٹا تو دونوں میں سے کسی پر قصاص نہیں ہے اور دونوں پر ایک ہاتھ کی آدھی آدھی دیت ہے۔
چونکہ دو آدمیوں نے ایک آدمی کا ایک ہاتھ کاٹا ہے اس لئے بدلے میں دونوں کے دو ہاتھ کاٹے نہیں جائیں گے۔ ورنہ تعدی اور زیادتی ہو جائے گی۔ اور کسی ایک کا ہاتھ نہیں کاٹ سکتے کہ ترجیح بلا مر ج ہوگی۔ اس لئے یہی صورت ہے کہ دونوں پر ملا کر ایک ہاتھ کی دیت لازم کریں۔ اور دونوں پر آدھی آدھی دیت ہوگی (۲) اثر میں ہے۔ عن الشعبی ان رجلين اتيا عليا فشهدا على رجل انه سرق فقطع على يده ثم اتـیـاه بآخر فقالا هذا الذي سرق واخطأنا على الاول فلم يجز شهادتهما على الآخر غرمهما دية يد حاشیہ : (الف) اس نفس کو مت قتل کرو جس کو اللہ نے حرام کیا ہے مگر حق کے ساتھ اور جو مظلوم قتل کیا گیا تو ہم نے اس کے ولی کے لئے قوت دی تو قتل میں زیادتی نہ کرے وہ مدد کیا ہوا ہے۔  
اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ حضرت علیؓ نے ایک ہاتھ کی دیت لازم کی۔ اس لئے دونوں پر آدھی آدھی دیت لازم ہوگی۔ 
امام شافعی فرماتے ہیں کہ دونوں کے ہاتھ کاٹے جائیں گے۔ اوپر کے اثر سے استدلال ہے۔ اس میں ہے۔ قال لا اعلمكما تعمد تما لقطعتكما (سن للبیہقی ، باب الاثنين اوا کثر قطعان ید رجل معاج ثامن، ص ۷۵ نمبر ۱۵۹۷۷ار بخاری شریف نمبر ۶۸۹۶) اس اثر میں ہے کہ اگر جانتا کہ جان بوجھ کر کاٹے ہو تو دونوں کا ہاتھ کا تھا۔ جس سے معلوم ہوا کہ قصاص میں دونوں کے ہاتھ کاٹے جائیں گے۔
[2332] (39) And if two men cut the hand of one man, there is no qisas on each of them, and upon both is half the diyah. This verse indicates that the penalty of one person does not fall on others. [2332] (39) If two men cut the hand of one man, there is no qisas on either of them, and on both is half-half diyah of one hand. Since two men cut one hand of one man, both of their two hands cannot be cut in retaliation, otherwise it would be transgression and excess. And one person's hand cannot be cut because that would be preference without cause. So the only possibility is that the diyah of one hand is obligatory on both collectively, and each owes half. (2) There is a narration: From al-Sha'bi: Two men came to 'Ali and testified that a man had stolen, so 'Ali cut off his hand. Then they brought another and said, 'This one stole, and we made a mistake about the first.' So 'Ali did not accept their testimony against the second and fined them the diyah of the hand. (Footnote: This verse...). From this narration it is understood that 'Ali made the diyah of one hand obligatory. Therefore, each owes half diyah. Imam al-Shafi'i says that both their hands should be cut. He argues from the above narration: 'He said: If I knew you did it deliberately, I would have cut both your hands.' (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, Bab al-ithnayn...). In this narration, it is that if he knew they had intentionally cut, he would have cut both hands. This indicates that in qisas, both hands should be cut.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage presents a Hanafi ruling on joint offenders in retributive justice for bodily injury. The matn states: if two men cut the hand of one man, neither is subject to qisas (amputation), and both share the diyah (blood money) of one hand equally. The Urdu commentary explains the reasoning: cutting both offenders' hands would be excessive (since the victim lost only one hand), and cutting only one offender's hand would be arbitrary. Therefore, the only just solution is to impose monetary compensation—the diyah of one hand—split evenly. The text then provides a historical proof: a narration from al-Sha'bi about 'Ali ibn Abi Talib. In that case, two witnesses falsely accused a man of theft, 'Ali cut off his hand, then they admitted error and accused another. 'Ali fined them the diyah of the hand, not a hand-for-hand retaliation. This is used by the Hanafi school to support their position. The passage then mentions the Shafi'i position: Imam al-Shafi'i holds that both offenders should undergo qisas (i.e., both hands amputated). He cites the same narration but focuses on 'Ali's statement: 'If I knew you did it deliberately, I would have cut off both your hands.' The Hanafi school interprets this as 'Ali's hypothetical statement, but they maintain that the actual judgment was only diyah, and that qisas for multiple offenders in a single hand is impermissible due to the principle of proportionality. The passage thus presents a clear inter-madhhab dispute.

Key Terms
قصاص (qisas): Retributive justice; here specifically amputation of the hand in retaliation for cutting.
دية اليد (diyat al-yad): Blood money for the hand; amount varies (typically half the full diyah in Hanafi school).
ترجيح بلا مرجح (tarjih bila murajjih): Preference without a preponderating factor; invalid in law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the narration from al-Sha'bi about 'Ali, what did 'Ali impose on the two false witnesses?
A) Qisas of the hand
B) Diyah of the hand
C) Both
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
When multiple offenders cause a single hand amputation, the Hanafi school imposes shared diyah, not qisas, to maintain proportionality and avoid arbitrary selection.

Qisas — Retaliation
intermediate
6 min
Shared Qisas for Multiple Victims of a Single Limb
Ruling on sharing qisas when one limb is cut from two victims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on qisas when one offender cuts the same limb of two victims, including the division of diyah.

Core Idea
One right hand replaces two: qisas is shared, diyah fills the gap.

This lesson addresses a specific qisas scenario: one person cuts the right hands of two victims. Since the offender has only one right hand, full retaliation for both is impossible. The Hanafi school provides a balanced solution: shared qisas and proportional diyah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and injury
The offender cut the right hands of two different victims. Each victim has a right to qisas for his right hand.
The injury must be exactly the same limb (right hand) for this rule to apply.
2
Determine available qisas
The offender has only one right hand, so qisas can be carried out only once.
3
Apply the sharing rule when both victims are present
If both victims come together, they collectively cut the offender's right hand. This satisfies one victim's qisas. The other victim receives half diyah (half the diyah of a hand, i.e., 25 camels) which is then split equally between both victims.
The half diyah is taken from the offender and divided equally, so each gets 25 camels.
4
Analyze the separate scenario
If one victim acts alone and cuts the hand, the other victim is only entitled to half diyah from the offender (since the hand is already used for qisas).
The second victim cannot demand a second qisas on a different limb.
5
State the underlying principle
When full qisas is impossible for all claimants, the available qisas is executed, and the shortfall is compensated by proportional diyah.
This principle is derived from the broader rule: when qisas cannot be taken, diyah is taken.

Source Text

[۲۳۳۳] (۴۰) ایک آدمی نے دو آدمیوں کے دائیں ہاتھوں کو کاٹا۔ پس دونوں آئیں تو دونوں کو حق ہے کہ اس کے ایک ہاتھ کاٹ لے اور اس سے آدھی دیت لے ۔ جس کو آپس میں آدھی آدمی تقسیم کرنے۔

[۲۳۳۴] (۴۱) ان میں سے ایک آیا اور اس کا ہاتھ کاٹ لیا تو دوسرے کے لئے اس پر آدھی دیت ہے۔
[2333] (40) And if one cuts the right [hand] of two men, and both come, then they have the right to cut his hand and take from him half the diyah, dividing it into two halves. [2334] (41) If one of them comes and cuts his hand, then for the other is half the diyah. [2333] (40) One man cut the right hands of two men. So when both come, both have the right to cut off his one hand and take from him half the diyah, which they divide between themselves half-half. One man cut the right hands of two men. Now the cutter does not have two right hands so that they could be cut. And here there is the right of two men's right hands. So both have the right to cut the cutter's right hand, whereby both victims will receive half their right, and for the remaining half right, they take from the cutter the diyah of one hand which is half the diyah, i.e., fifty camels, and divide it half-half, i.e., twenty-five camels each. One man's right hand is compensated by the cutter's right hand, and the other man's right hand is compensated by half diyah. Because the cutter does not have another right hand to be cut. And the left hand cannot be cut because he cut the right hands of both. So the only remaining possibility is to take the diyah of the hand, which is half the full diyah, and both share half-half. (2) It has passed earlier that if qisas cannot be taken, then diyah is taken. [2334] (41) If one of them comes and cuts his hand, then for the other is half the diyah against him.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific scenario in qisas for limbs: one person cuts the right hand of two different victims. The matn gives the ruling: if both victims come forward together, they may collectively retaliate by cutting the offender's one right hand, and then take from him half the diyah (blood money) of a hand, which they divide equally. The Urdu commentary explains the reasoning: the offender has only one right hand, so he cannot give two right hands in qisas. Therefore, the victims share the available right hand: one victim's right is satisfied by the amputation, and the other receives half diyah (since full compensation for a right hand is half the full diyah). This half diyah (e.g., 50 camels) is then divided equally between both victims, each getting 25 camels. The left hand of the offender cannot be cut because the injuries were to right hands, and proportionality requires matching right for right. The principle applied is that when qisas is impossible (due to insufficient body parts), diyah is substituted. The second part (41) covers the scenario where one victim acts alone and cuts the offender's hand before the other victim arrives. In that case, the first victim has taken his share (qisas), and the second victim is entitled to half diyah from the offender (since the offender's right hand is already used). The commentary notes that this is consistent with the general rule: when qisas cannot be fully executed, diyah is paid.

Key Terms
يمنى (yumna): Right (hand); specifically the right hand as opposed to the left.
نصف الدية (nisf al-diyah): Half the blood money; here, half the diyah of a hand (which itself is half the full diyah).
يقتسمانها نصفين (yaqtasimānihā nisfayn): They divide it into two halves; used to describe the equal division of the diyah between two victims.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
When one offender cuts the right hands of two victims, qisas is executed on the single right hand, and the deficit is compensated by half diyah shared equally.
